أما الأول فظاهر. وأما الثاني : فلأن الجزء داخل في ماهية الكل، وليس بداخل في ماهية نفسه، ومبنى اللزوم على أن الوجود المطلق الذي فرض التركيب فيه ليس خارجاً عن الوجودات الخاصة، بل إما نفس ماهيتها ليلزم الثاني، أو جزء مقوم لها ليلزم الأول، وإلا فيجوز أن تكون الأجزاء وجودات خاصة نفس الماهيات أو زائدة عليها، والمطلق خارج عنها فلا يلزم شيء من المحالين، وإن لم تكن الأجزاء وجودات فإما أن يحصل عند اجتماعهما أمر زائد يكون هو الوجود أو لا يحصل، فإن لم يحصل كان الوجود محض ما ليس بوجود وهو محال، وإن حصل لم يكن التركيب في الوجود الذي هو نفس ذلك الزائد العارض بل في معروضه هذا خلف. وتقرير الإمام في المباحث أنه لو تركب الوجود فأجزاؤه إن كانت وجودية كان الوجود الواحد وجودات، وإن لم تكن وجودية فإن لم يحدث لها عند اجتماعها صفة الوجود كان الوجود عبارة من مجموع الأمور العدمية، وإن حدثت يكون ذلك المجموع مؤثراً في ذلك الوجود أو قابلاً له فلا يكون التركيب في نفس الوجود بل في قابله أو فاعله. وأما بالرسم فلما سبق من أنه يفيد بعد العلم باختصاص الخارج بالمرسوم وهذا متوقف على العلم به وهو دور وبما عداه مفصلاً وهو محال، ولو سلم فلا يفيد معرفة الحقيقة. والجواب عن التقرير الأول لدليل امتناع تركب الوجود النقض أي لو صح بجميع مقدماته لزم أن لا يكون شيء من الماهيات مركباً لجريانه فيها بأن يقال أجزاء البيت إما بيوت وهو محال وإما غير بيوت وحينئذ إما أن يحصل عند اجتماعها أمر زائد هو البيت فلا يكون التركيب في البيت هذا خلف أو لا يحصل فيكون البيت محض ما ليس ببيت. والحل بأنا نختار أنه يحصل أمر زائد على كل جزء وهو المجموع الذي هو نفس الوجود فلا يكون التركيب إلا فيه ولا حاجة إلى حصول أمر زائد على المجموع، فالوجود محض المجموع الذي ليس شيء من أجزائه بوجود، كما أن البيت محض الأجسام التي ليس شيء منها ببيت والعشرة محض الأحاد التي شيء منها بعشرة. فإن قيل : هذا إنما يستقيم في الأجزاء الخارجية، وكلامنا في الأجزاء العقلية التي يقع بها التحديد إلزاماً لمن اعترف بزيادة الوجود على الماهية، إذ ليس على القول بالاشتراك اللفظي وجود مطلق، يدعي بداهته، أو اكتسابه، بل له معان بعضها بديهي وبعضها كسبي، وحينئذ لا يصح الحل بأن أجزاء الوجود أمور تتصف بالعدم أو بوجود هو عين الماهية أو لا تتصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم. قلنا : فالحل ما أشرنا إليه من أنها وجودات أي أمور يصدق عليها الوجود صدق العارض على المعروض، وحينئذ لا يلزم شيء من المحالين، ولا اتصاف الشيء بالوجود قبل تحقق الوجود، لأنه لا تمايز بين الجنس، والفصل، والنوع إلا بحسب العقل دون الخارج. فمعنى قولنا يكون الوجود محض ما ليس شيء من أجزائه بوجود أنه لا يكون شيء من الأجزاء نفس الوجود، وإن كان يصدق عليه الوجود كسائر المركبات بالنسبة إلى الأجزاء العقلية فإنها لا تكون نفس ذلك المركب لكنه يصدق عليها صدق العارض.
As for the first, it is clear. As for the second: because the part is included in the quiddity of the whole, but is not included in its own quiddity, and the basis of the entailment is that absolute existence, in which composition is supposed, is not external to particular existences; rather it is either the very quiddity of them, so that the second [impossibility] follows, or a constituent part of them, so that the first follows. Otherwise, it is possible that the parts are particular existences that are the quiddities themselves or superadded to them, and the absolute is external to them, so neither of the two impossibilities follows. And if the parts are not existences, then either when they are combined an additional thing occurs which is existence, or it does not occur. If it does not occur, then existence would be a mere thing that is not existence, which is impossible. And if it occurs, then composition is not in existence, which is that additional accident, but in its substrate — this is a contradiction. The report of the Imam in al-Mabahith: if existence were composite, then its parts: if they are existential, then one existence would be [multiple] existences; and if they are not existential, then if the attribute of existence does not arise in them upon their combination, existence would consist of a sum of non-existential things; and if it does arise, then that sum would be an effect or receptacle for that existence, so composition would not be in existence itself but in its receptacle or agent. As for the definition by description, it has preceded that it gives knowledge after knowledge of the exclusivity of the external to the described, and this depends on knowledge of it, which is circular, and knowledge of everything else in detail is impossible; and even if conceded, it does not give knowledge of the essence. The answer to the first report of the proof of the impossibility of the composition of existence is a counter-argument: if it were sound with all its premises, then nothing among quiddities would be composite, because the same reasoning applies to them, namely: the parts of a house are either houses — which is impossible — or they are not houses, and then either upon their combination an additional thing occurs which is the house, so composition is not in the house — this is a contradiction — or it does not occur, so the house would be a mere thing that is not a house. The solution is that we choose that an additional thing occurs over and above each part, which is the whole that is existence itself, so composition is only in it and there is no need for something additional to the whole. Thus existence is a mere whole none of whose parts is an existence, just as the house is a mere body none of whose parts is a house, and ten is a mere units none of whose parts is ten. If it is said: this only holds for external parts, and our discussion is about the intellectual parts by which definition is made, as an objection against those who affirm the superaddition of existence to quiddity; since according to the position of verbal equivocation there is no absolute existence that one claims to be self-evident or acquired, rather it has meanings some self-evident and some acquired; and then the solution that parts of existence are things that are qualified by non-existence or by an existence that is identical to quiddity, or are not qualified by either existence or non-existence, is not valid. We say: the solution is what we have indicated, that they are existences, i.e., things to which existence applies as an accident applies to its substrate; and then neither of the impossibilities follows, and there is no qualification of a thing with existence before the realization of existence, because there is no distinction between genus, differentia, and species except according to the intellect, not externally. So the meaning of our statement 'existence is a mere thing none of whose parts is an existence' is that none of the parts is existence itself, even if existence applies to it as an accident, like other composites with respect to intellectual parts: they are not that composite itself, but existence applies to them as an accident.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage is a complex refutation of the objection that existence cannot be composite. It begins with the author's own argument (the first point is clear, the second point involves the part being inside the whole). Then it presents the objection from Imam al-Razi's al-Mabahith: if existence were composite, its parts would either be existences (leading to one existence being many existences) or non-existences (leading to existence being a sum of non-existences or requiring a substrate). The author then answers by first giving a counter-argument (naqd) using the example of a house: if the argument were sound, no composite quiddity would exist. He then provides the solution (hall): existence is a whole whose parts are not existences themselves but are things to which existence applies accidentally. A further objection is raised that this only works for external parts, not intellectual parts used in definitions, especially against those who hold existence as superadded. The author responds that the solution is the same: the parts are 'existences' in the sense that existence is predicated of them accidentally, just as in any intellectual composite. The key nuance is that existential predicates do not require the parts to be existence itself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the counter-argument (naqd) proves the impossible is true, but the author uses it to show the original argument is flawed by reductio ad absurdum.
Correction: The naqd does not claim that houses are impossible; it shows that if the objection's reasoning were valid, houses would be impossible, which is false, so the reasoning is invalid.

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of the house example in the counter-argument?
A) To show that houses are simple entities.
B) To prove that existence cannot be composite.
C) To demonstrate that the objection's reasoning would absurdly rule out all composites, including houses.
D) To illustrate that parts of a house are themselves houses.

Key Takeaway
The counter-argument (naqd) uses a parallel example to show that a flawed argument leads to absurd consequences, thus refuting it, while the solution clarifies that existence is a whole whose parts are not existences themselves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage distinguishes between the whole and its parts, emphasizing that the whole is not a part. In your own understanding of your identity, do you confuse the whole of who you are with one of your parts—like your career, your status, or your sins? How does this confusion affect your relationship with Allah, who knows you as a whole?
?
The author uses a counter-argument (naqd) to show that an opposing view leads to absurdity. Consider a doubt you have about your faith or a sin you struggle with. Does that doubt or sin, when followed to its logical end, lead to an absurd or harmful conclusion? Are you willing to use the same rational scrutiny to dismantle it and submit to the truth of the Quran and Sunnah?
?
The solution demonstrates that existence can be composite without its parts being existences themselves. Reflect on the fact that your own existence is contingent—made up of atoms, moments, and experiences that are not 'you' in themselves. How does this contingency drive you to rely on the Necessary Existent, Allah, alone?

The Solution: Accidental Predication of Existence to Intellectual Parts
The author answers the objection concerning intellectual parts and clarifies accidental predication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends his solution against the objection about intellectual parts, and how existence can be accidentally predicated of the parts of a composite.

Core Idea
Existence is predicated accidentally of the parts, just as 'house' is predicated of its parts.

In the previous lesson, the author gave a counter-argument and a solution using external composites. But the objector raises a new issue: his argument was about intellectual parts used in definitions, not external parts. The author responds by showing that the same accidental predication works for intellectual parts as well. This lesson completes the refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection about intellectual parts
Read the passage beginning with 'فإن قيل' which raises the objection that the solution only works for external parts, but the discussion is about intellectual parts that define quiddities.
This is a common move: distinguishing between external and intellectual composition.
2
Understand the author's response
The author replies that the solution is the same: the intellectual parts are 'existences' in the sense that existence is accidentally predicated of them, just like any composite.
He uses the example of genus, differentia, and species: they are distinct only in the mind, not externally.
3
Clarify the meaning of 'mere whole'
The author explains his earlier statement: 'existence is a mere whole none of whose parts is existence' means that no part is existence itself, but existence applies to them accidentally.
This avoids the objection that parts would need existence before composition.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider how the author has defended the composability of existence against the Imam's objection. The key is the distinction between essential and accidental predication.
This is a sophisticated point in kalam ontology.

أما الأول فظاهر. وأما الثاني : فلأن الجزء داخل في ماهية الكل، وليس بداخل في ماهية نفسه، ومبنى اللزوم على أن الوجود المطلق الذي فرض التركيب فيه ليس خارجاً عن الوجودات الخاصة، بل إما نفس ماهيتها ليلزم الثاني، أو جزء مقوم لها ليلزم الأول، وإلا فيجوز أن تكون الأجزاء وجودات خاصة نفس الماهيات أو زائدة عليها، والمطلق خارج عنها فلا يلزم شيء من المحالين، وإن لم تكن الأجزاء وجودات فإما أن يحصل عند اجتماعهما أمر زائد يكون هو الوجود أو لا يحصل، فإن لم يحصل كان الوجود محض ما ليس بوجود وهو محال، وإن حصل لم يكن التركيب في الوجود الذي هو نفس ذلك الزائد العارض بل في معروضه هذا خلف. وتقرير الإمام في المباحث أنه لو تركب الوجود فأجزاؤه إن كانت وجودية كان الوجود الواحد وجودات، وإن لم تكن وجودية فإن لم يحدث لها عند اجتماعها صفة الوجود كان الوجود عبارة من مجموع الأمور العدمية، وإن حدثت يكون ذلك المجموع مؤثراً في ذلك الوجود أو قابلاً له فلا يكون التركيب في نفس الوجود بل في قابله أو فاعله. وأما بالرسم فلما سبق من أنه يفيد بعد العلم باختصاص الخارج بالمرسوم وهذا متوقف على العلم به وهو دور وبما عداه مفصلاً وهو محال، ولو سلم فلا يفيد معرفة الحقيقة. والجواب عن التقرير الأول لدليل امتناع تركب الوجود النقض أي لو صح بجميع مقدماته لزم أن لا يكون شيء من الماهيات مركباً لجريانه فيها بأن يقال أجزاء البيت إما بيوت وهو محال وإما غير بيوت وحينئذ إما أن يحصل عند اجتماعها أمر زائد هو البيت فلا يكون التركيب في البيت هذا خلف أو لا يحصل فيكون البيت محض ما ليس ببيت. والحل بأنا نختار أنه يحصل أمر زائد على كل جزء وهو المجموع الذي هو نفس الوجود فلا يكون التركيب إلا فيه ولا حاجة إلى حصول أمر زائد على المجموع، فالوجود محض المجموع الذي ليس شيء من أجزائه بوجود، كما أن البيت محض الأجسام التي ليس شيء منها ببيت والعشرة محض الأحاد التي شيء منها بعشرة. فإن قيل : هذا إنما يستقيم في الأجزاء الخارجية، وكلامنا في الأجزاء العقلية التي يقع بها التحديد إلزاماً لمن اعترف بزيادة الوجود على الماهية، إذ ليس على القول بالاشتراك اللفظي وجود مطلق، يدعي بداهته، أو اكتسابه، بل له معان بعضها بديهي وبعضها كسبي، وحينئذ لا يصح الحل بأن أجزاء الوجود أمور تتصف بالعدم أو بوجود هو عين الماهية أو لا تتصف بالوجود ولا بالعدم. قلنا : فالحل ما أشرنا إليه من أنها وجودات أي أمور يصدق عليها الوجود صدق العارض على المعروض، وحينئذ لا يلزم شيء من المحالين، ولا اتصاف الشيء بالوجود قبل تحقق الوجود، لأنه لا تمايز بين الجنس، والفصل، فإنها لا تكون نفس ذلك المركب لكنه يصدق عليها صدق العارض.

Answering the Second Objection: Parts of Existence as Existential
The author answers the second objection regarding the composition of existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author defends the view that the parts of existence are existential without implying that one existence becomes many.

Core Idea
Parts are existential by attribution, not identity.

In this passage, the author addresses the second objection against the composition of existence. The objector claimed that if the parts of existence are themselves existential, then the one existence would fragment into many existences. The author responds by clarifying the relationship between existence and the existent, showing that multiplicity arises only in the mind, not in reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact style of argument.
The passage is a direct response to an objection.
2
Identify the objection and the author's response
The objector says: if parts are existential, one existence becomes many. The author first affirms the parts are existential, then denies the entailment.
Focus on the phrase 'we choose' and 'we do not concede'.
3
Understand the conditional logic
The author explains that the entailment would only hold if existence were identical to the essence. Since he holds existence is accidental, the objection fails.
This is a key distinction in kalam.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The multiplicity is intellectual, not real, and not impossible, like other composites of intellectual parts.
Compare with examples like genus and species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والجواب عن التقرير الثاني : أنا نختار أن أجزاء الوجود وجوديات، ولا نسلم لزوم كون الوجود الواحد وجودات، وإنما يلزم لو كان وجود الوجودي عينه ولو فيكون الوجود الواحد في نفس الأمر وجودات بحسب العقل، ولا استحالة فيه كما في سائر المركبات من الأجزاء العقلية.
And the answer to the second report: we choose that the parts of existence are existential, and we do not concede the entailment that one existence becomes [multiple] existences; it would only follow if the existence of the existential was identical to it, and then the one existence would be in reality [multiple] existences according to the intellect, and there is no impossibility in that, as in the case of other composites of intellectual parts.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is the answer to the second objection (al-taqrīr al-thānī) regarding the composition of existence. The objector argued that if the parts of existence are existential (wujūdiyyāt), then one existence would be multiple existences. The author responds by first affirming that the parts are indeed existential. He then denies the entailment: it would only follow if the existence of an existential thing were identical to that thing itself (i.e., if existence were the same as the essence). But if existence is accidental to the quiddity, then calling the parts 'existential' does not mean they are existence itself; rather, they are things to which existence applies. Thus, the one existence can be said to be multiple existences only in an intellectual sense (according to the mind), and this is not impossible, just as in other composites of intellectual parts (e.g., a genus and differentia are both concepts, but the species is one).

وجود
وجوديات
لزوم
وجود الواحد
أجزاء عقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If the parts of existence are existential, then the one existence becomes many existences in reality.
Correction: The author explains that this would only follow if existence were identical to the essence, but here existence is accidental, so the multiplicity is only in the mind.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why does saying 'the parts of existence are existential' not entail that one existence becomes multiple existences?
A) Because the parts are non-existent.
B) Because the parts are not existence itself but are attributed by existence.
C) Because existence cannot have parts.
D) Because the objector's argument is invalid due to a logical fallacy.

Key Takeaway
The parts of existence are existential by attribution, not identity, so the one existence does not become many in reality.

Self-Evidence of the Conception of Existence
Distinguishing concept from judgment about self-evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the self-evidence of the conception of existence does not imply the self-evidence of the judgment that it is self-evident.

Core Idea
Self-evidence of a concept does not make the judgment about it self-evident.

The author begins by clarifying a common confusion: just because we have an immediate conception of existence does not mean that the claim 'this conception is self-evident' is itself self-evident. This distinction is crucial for evaluating objections against the conceivability of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice how the author distinguishes between the concept and the judgment.
Pay attention to the two possibilities: acquired or hidden self-evident.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage states that the judgment about self-evidence can be either acquired (needing proof) or hidden self-evident (needing an alert). Understand why each requires a different approach.
3
Ask: why does this matter?
This distinction allows the author to argue that objections against the self-evidence of existence may be based on a misunderstanding of the judgment itself.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواضح ، وبداهة تصور الوجود لا تستلزم بداهة الحكم بأنه بديهي، فيجوز أن يكون هذا الحكم كسبياً أو بديهياً خفياً لا يكون في حكم قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين فيقع فيه الاختلاف، ويحتاج على الأول إلى الدليل، وعلى الثاني إلى التنبيه ويكون ما ذكر في معرض الاستدلال تنبيهات وقد يقال الوجود لا يتصور أصلاً وهو مكابرة في مقابلة القول بأنه أظهر الأشياء واخترع الإمام لذلك تمسكات منها : أنه لو كان متصوراً لكان الواجب متصوراً إلزاماً للقائلين بأن حقيقة الوجود المجرد ، ومعنى التجرد معلوم قطعا ومبناه على أن الوجود طبيعة نوعية لا تختلف إلا بالإضافات وليس كذلك على ما سيأتي ، ومنها أنه لو تصور رتسم في النفس صورة مساوية له مع أن للنفس وجوداً فيجتمع مثلان . والجواب : منع التماثل بين وجود النفس والصورة الكلية للوجود على أن الممتنع من اجتماع المثلين هو قيامهما بمحل واحد كقيام العرض وههنا لو سلم قيام الصورة كذلك فظاهر، أن ليس قيام الوجود كذلك لما سيجيء من أن زيادة الوجود على الماهية إنما هي في الذهن فقط . لا وأما الجواب : بأنه يكفي لتصور الوجود وجود النفس كما يكفي لتصور ذاتها نفس ذاتها فإنما يصح على رأي من يجعل الوجود حقيقة واحدة لا يختلف إلا بالإضافة وإلا فكيف يكفي لتصور الوجود المطلق حصول الوجود الخاص الذي هو معروض لها، ومنها أن تصوره بالحقيقة لا يكون إلا إذا علم تميزه عما عداه، بمعنى أنه ليس غيره وهذا سلب مخصوص لا يعقل إلا بعد تعقل السلب المطلق، وهو نفي صرف لا يعقل إلا بالإضافة إلى وجود فيدور. والجواب - أن تصوره يتوقف على تميزه لا على العلم بتميزه ولو سلم فالسلب المخصوص إنما يتوقف تعقله على تعقل السلب المطلق لو كان ذاتياً له وهو ممنوع ولو سلم فلا نسلم أن النفي الصرف لا يعقل، ولو سلم فالسلب يضاف إلى الإيجاب وهو غير الموجود. (۱) في (أ) بزيادة لفظ (واحد) .
The clear [thing] is that the self-evidence of the conception of existence does not necessitate the self-evidence of the judgment that it is self-evident, so it is possible that this judgment is acquired or a hidden self-evident [truth] that is not like our statement 'one is half of two', so disagreement occurs in it, and on the first [case] it needs a proof, and on the second [it needs] an alert, and what is mentioned in the context of inference are alerts. And it may be said that existence is not conceived at all, which is obstinacy in opposition to the statement that it is the most manifest of things. The Imam invented for that [claim] some arguments: among them, that if it were conceived, then the Necessary [Existent] would be conceived, as an implication for those who say that the reality of pure existence and the meaning of purity are known definitively, and it is based on the fact that existence is a generic nature which does not differ except by relations, but it is not so as will come. And among them, that if it were conceived, a form equal to it would be imprinted in the soul while the soul has an existence, so two similars would come together. The answer: prevention of similarity between the existence of the soul and the universal form of existence, for the impossibility of the coming together of two similars is their subsisting in one locus, like the subsistence of an accident, and here if the subsistence of the form is conceded likewise, it is apparent that the subsistence of existence is not likewise, as will come about that the increment of existence over quiddity is only in the mind. No. And as for the answer that the existence of the soul suffices for conceiving existence just as its own essence suffices for conceiving its essence, that is only valid according to the opinion of one who makes existence a single reality that does not differ except by relation, otherwise how would the attainment of specific existence which is the substrate for it suffice for conceiving absolute existence? And among them, that its true conception is only when its distinction from what is other than it is known, meaning that it is not other, and this is a specific negation which is only intellectual after the intellectual [grasp] of absolute negation, and it is a pure negation which is only intellectual by relation to an existence, so there is circularity. The answer: its conception depends on its distinction, not on the knowledge of its distinction; and if conceded, the specific negation only depends its being intellectual on the intellect [grasp] of absolute negation if it is essential to it, which is prevented; and if conceded, we do not concede that pure negation is not intellectual; and if conceded, negation is related to affirmation, and it is non-existent. (1) In (A) with an addition of the word 'waahid'.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage discusses objections regarding the conception of existence. The author begins by noting that the self-evidence of the conception of existence does not imply the self-evidence of the judgment that it is self-evident; the judgment may be acquired or a hidden self-evident truth, requiring proof or alert. He then presents objections attributed to the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) against the conceivability of existence, each followed by an answer. The first objection: if existence were conceivable, the Necessary Existent would be conceivable, but that is not the case. The answer: this objection relies on the view that existence is a generic nature, which the author rejects. The second objection: conceiving existence would result in a mental image similar to existence, leading to two similars coexisting. The answer: two similars cannot coexist in one substrate, and the image and existence do not share the same substrate. Another objection: that the existence of the soul suffices to conceive existence is only valid if existence is a single reality differing only by relation; otherwise, a specific existence cannot represent absolute existence. The final objection: conception requires knowledge of distinction from others, leading to circularity. The answer: conception depends on distinction itself, not on knowledge of it; furthermore, absolute negation is conceivable. The passage ends with a textual note on a variant reading.

Key Terms
الوجود
بديهي
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a concept is self-evident, then the statement that it is self-evident is also self-evident.
Correction: The conception and the judgment about it are distinct; the judgment may require proof or reflection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two possibilities for the judgment that the conception of existence is self-evident?
A) Necessarily true or necessarily false
B) Acquired or hidden self-evident
C) Obvious or obscure
D) Proof-based or intuition-based

Key Takeaway
The self-evidence of a concept does not automatically make the judgment about it self-evident; the judgment may require proof or alert.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you assumed that because a concept feels clear to you, your judgment about it must also be automatically correct? How did that assumption prevent you from examining deeper truths about your faith?
?
The author says the judgment about self-evidence may be a 'hidden self-evident' that needs an alert. What hidden truth about your relationship with Allah have you been ignoring because it hasn't been made obvious to you? Will you actively seek that alert now?
?
If even the judgment about existence's self-evidence can require proof or reminder, how much more so do the deeper matters of your soul need careful investigation? What step will you take today to move beyond assumptions and seek certitude about what you believe?

Allah's Existence

Objection One: Conceiving Existence Implies Conceiving the Necessary Existent
First objection and its refutation by denying existence as a generic nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first objection against conceiving existence and the author's refutation based on the nature of existence.

Core Idea
The objection fails because it assumes existence is a generic nature, which the author denies.

Imam al-Razi's first objection argues that if existence were conceivable, then the Necessary Existent (God) would also be conceivable, which is problematic. The author responds by rejecting the underlying premise that existence is a generic nature that applies uniformly to all beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Identify the key claim: that conceiving existence entails conceiving the Necessary Existent.
Notice the phrase 'إلزاماً للقائلين' – it is an implication directed at those who hold a certain view.
2
Identify the underlying premise
The objection depends on the view that existence is a generic nature (طبيعة نوعية) that only differs by relations. Understand why this premise is crucial.
3
Study the answer
The author dismisses the objection by stating that existence is not such a generic nature, as will be explained later. Consider how this refutation works.
The phrase 'وليس كذلك على ما سيأتي' indicates a future argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever tried to fully define or conceptualize God in your mind, perhaps to feel closer to Him? After reading this lesson, do you see how reducing God to a concept might actually limit your understanding of His absolute transcendence? Will you commit to accepting that He is beyond complete human conception, and adjust your worship accordingly?
?
This lesson shows a logical move to protect God's uniqueness by denying existence as a generic nature. In your daily thoughts, do you unconsciously treat God as just another 'existing thing' among others, even if the most powerful? How can you train yourself to remember that His existence is radically different from all other existents?
?
The author implies that your mental picture of God is not equivalent to God's reality. Are you willing to let go of the comfort of a God you can fully 'grasp' and instead embrace a relationship with a God who is infinitely beyond your imagination? What would that mean for your prayer life today?

Objection Two: Conceiving Existence Leads to Two Similars
Second objection and its refutation via substrate distinction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection about two similars and the author's response regarding substrate and the nature of mental existence.

Core Idea
Two similars cannot coexist in one substrate, and the mental image and extra-mental existence have different substrates.

The second objection argues that conceiving existence would create a mental form equal to existence, leading to two similars (the soul's existence and the mental form). The author counters by distinguishing the substrate of the mental image from that of extra-mental existence, and also addresses a related answer about the soul's existence suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the entire passage for this objection. Identify the core claim: that conceiving existence leads to two similars coexisting.
The objection assumes the mental image is similar to the external existence.
2
Understand the refutation
The author argues that two similars cannot coexist in one substrate. The mental image and the soul's existence are not in the same substrate. Also, the additional answer about the soul's existence is only valid if existence is a single reality.
Pay attention to the phrase 'قيامهما بمحل واحد'.
3
Examine the counter-answer
The author also refutes a potential counter-answer: that the soul's existence suffices to conceive existence. He says this only works if existence is a single reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A mental image of existence is exactly similar to extra-mental existence and they can coexist in one substrate.
Correction: The mental image and extra-mental existence are not similar in their mode of being and do not share the same substrate.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main reason for denying that two similars arise from conceiving existence?
A) Two similars cannot subsist in one locus
B) The mental form is not a true existence
C) The soul has no existence
D) Existence is not conceivable

Key Takeaway
The second objection fails because the mental image and the soul's existence are not similar and do not occupy the same substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you imagine or conceptualize Allah's existence, do you ever assume that your mental image is somehow equal to or a direct replica of His actual reality? How does this lesson's distinction between the mental form and the soul's existence challenge that assumption, and what must you do to guard against such a confusion in your worship?
?
The author rejects the idea that a specific existence (like your own soul's existence) can adequately represent absolute existence. In your spiritual life, do you sometimes rely on your personal experiences or feelings as a measure of Allah's truth? How can you avoid making your subjective states a standard for Allah's objective reality, and instead submit to what He has revealed about Himself?
?
The objection relies on a logical principle about similars. Have you ever allowed a philosophical dilemma or doubt to shake your certainty in a clear revealed truth? How can you apply the method of careful distinction (like distinguishing substrates) to resolve such doubts and strengthen your faith in Allah's oneness and incomparability?

Objection Three: Circularity in Conceiving Existence
Third objection about circularity and its refutation by redefining the requirements for conception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection regarding circularity and the author's response by distinguishing distinction from knowledge of distinction.

Core Idea
Conception of existence depends on distinction itself, not on knowledge of distinction, breaking the circularity.

The third objection argues that true conception of existence requires knowing its distinction from others, which leads to circularity because distinction presupposes negation, and negation presupposes existence. The author responds by clarifying that conception depends on objective distinction, not on knowledge of distinction, and also challenges the assumptions about negation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic and translation of the objection. Identify the steps: conception requires distinction, distinction requires specific negation, specific negation requires absolute negation, absolute negation requires existence, leading to circularity.
Notice the terms 'سلب مخصوص' and 'سلب مطلق'.
2
Understand the circularity argument
The objection claims that to conceive existence, we must already know what it is not, which involves negation, but negation itself is only understood in relation to existence, creating a circle.
3
Study the author's refutation
The author rejects the need for knowledge of distinction; it suffices that existence is distinct. He also challenges the dependency of specific negation on absolute negation and the very intelligibility of pure negation.
The answer has multiple conditional concessions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواضح ، وبداهة تصور الوجود لا تستلزم بداهة الحكم بأنه بديهي، فيجوز أن يكون هذا الحكم كسبياً أو بديهياً خفياً لا يكون في حكم قولنا : الواحد نصف الاثنين فيقع فيه الاختلاف، ويحتاج على الأول إلى الدليل، وعلى الثاني إلى التنبيه ويكون ما ذكر في معرض الاستدلال تنبيهات وقد يقال الوجود لا يتصور أصلاً وهو مكابرة في مقابلة القول بأنه أظهر الأشياء واخترع الإمام لذلك تمسكات منها : أنه لو كان متصوراً لكان الواجب متصوراً إلزاماً للقائلين بأن حقيقة الوجود المجرد ، ومعنى التجرد معلوم قطعا ومبناه على أن الوجود طبيعة نوعية لا تختلف إلا بالإضافات وليس كذلك على ما سيأتي ، ومنها أنه لو تصور رتسم في النفس صورة مساوية له مع أن للنفس وجوداً فيجتمع مثلان . والجواب : منع التماثل بين وجود النفس والصورة الكلية للوجود على أن الممتنع من اجتماع المثلين هو قيامهما بمحل واحد كقيام العرض وههنا لو سلم قيام الصورة كذلك فظاهر، أن ليس قيام الوجود كذلك لما سيجيء من أن زيادة الوجود على الماهية إنما هي في الذهن فقط . لا وأما الجواب : بأنه يكفي لتصور الوجود وجود النفس كما يكفي لتصور ذاتها نفس ذاتها فإنما يصح على رأي من يجعل الوجود حقيقة واحدة لا يختلف إلا بالإضافة وإلا فكيف يكفي لتصور الوجود المطلق حصول الوجود الخاص الذي هو معروض لها، ومنها أن تصوره بالحقيقة لا يكون إلا إذا علم تميزه عما عداه، بمعنى أنه ليس غيره وهذا سلب مخصوص لا يعقل إلا بعد تعقل السلب المطلق، وهو نفي صرف لا يعقل إلا بالإضافة إلى وجود فيدور. والجواب - أن تصوره يتوقف على تميزه لا على العلم بتميزه ولو سلم فالسلب المخصوص إنما يتوقف تعقله على تعقل السلب المطلق لو كان ذاتياً له وهو ممنوع ولو سلم فلا نسلم أن النفي الصرف لا يعقل، ولو سلم فالسلب يضاف إلى الإيجاب وهو غير الموجود. (۱) في (أ) بزيادة لفظ (واحد) .
The clear [thing] is that the self-evidence of the conception of existence does not necessitate the self-evidence of the judgment that it is self-evident, so it is possible that this judgment is acquired or a hidden self-evident [truth] that is not like our statement 'one is half of two', so disagreement occurs in it, and on the first [case] it needs a proof, and on the second [it needs] an alert, and what is mentioned in the context of inference are alerts. And it may be said that existence is not conceived at all, which is obstinacy in opposition to the statement that it is the most manifest of things. The Imam invented for that [claim] some arguments: among them, that if it were conceived, then the Necessary [Existent] would be conceived, as an implication for those who say that the reality of pure existence and the meaning of purity are known definitively, and it is based on the fact that existence is a generic nature which does not differ except by relations, but it is not so as will come. And among them, that if it were conceived, a form equal to it would be imprinted in the soul while the soul has an existence, so two similars would come together. The answer: prevention of similarity between the existence of the soul and the universal form of existence, for the impossibility of the coming together of two similars is their subsisting in one locus, like the subsistence of an accident, and here if the subsistence of the form is conceded likewise, it is apparent that the subsistence of existence is not likewise, as will come about that the increment of existence over quiddity is only in the mind. No. And as for the answer that the existence of the soul suffices for conceiving existence just as its own essence suffices for conceiving its essence, that is only valid according to the opinion of one who makes existence a single reality that does not differ except by relation, otherwise how would the attainment of specific existence which is the substrate for it suffice for conceiving absolute existence? And among them, that its true conception is only when its distinction from what is other than it is known, meaning that it is not other, and this is a specific negation which is only intellectual after the intellectual [grasp] of absolute negation, and it is a pure negation which is only intellectual by relation to an existence, so there is circularity. The answer: its conception depends on its distinction, not on the knowledge of its distinction; and if conceded, the specific negation only depends its being intellectual on the intellect [grasp] of absolute negation if it is essential to it, which is prevented; and if conceded, we do not concede that pure negation is not intellectual; and if conceded, negation is related to affirmation, and it is non-existent. (1) In (A) with an addition of the word 'waahid'.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage discusses objections regarding the conception of existence. The author begins by noting that the self-evidence of the conception of existence does not imply the self-evidence of the judgment that it is self-evident; the judgment may be acquired or a hidden self-evident truth, requiring proof or alert. He then presents objections attributed to the Imam (likely Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) against the conceivability of existence, each followed by an answer. The first objection: if existence were conceivable, the Necessary Existent would be conceivable, but that is not the case. The answer: this objection relies on the view that existence is a generic nature, which the author rejects. The second objection: conceiving existence would result in a mental image similar to existence, leading to two similars coexisting. The answer: two similars cannot coexist in one substrate, and the image and existence do not share the same substrate. Another objection: that the existence of the soul suffices to conceive existence is only valid if existence is a single reality differing only by relation; otherwise, a specific existence cannot represent absolute existence. The final objection: conception requires knowledge of distinction from others, leading to circularity. The answer: conception depends on distinction itself, not on knowledge of it; furthermore, absolute negation is conceivable. The passage ends with a textual note on a variant reading.

Key Terms
السلب المطلق
السلب المخصوص
تعقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: To conceive existence, one must already know what it is not, which is impossible without circularity.
Correction: Conception requires only that existence is distinct, not that we know its distinction; the circularity argument relies on unnecessary assumptions about negation.

Checkpoint
What is the author's first response to the circularity objection?
A) Conception depends on distinction, not knowledge of distinction
B) Specific negation does not depend on absolute negation
C) Pure negation is unintelligible
D) Negation relates to affirmation

Key Takeaway
The third objection fails because conception does not require knowledge of distinction, and the dependencies among negations are not essential.

Existence as a Single Common Concept
The author argues that existence has one shared meaning across all existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments for existence having a single common meaning and the difference between the Ash'ari position and the majority of theologians.

Core Idea
Existence is one concept shared by all things, not just a word with multiple meanings.

In this passage, the author establishes that the concept of existence is shared among all existents, contrary to the view of some theologians. He provides two proofs and responds to objections, concluding that for the majority, existence is a single reality that takes on different modes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own flow come through.
Notice the structure: thesis, proofs, objection-response, sectarian report.
2
Identify the two proofs
The author gives two arguments for existence being a single concept. Find them and note how they work.
The first relies on certainty despite ignorance of specifics; the second on the valid division of existence.
3
Understand the objection and response
The author anticipates an objection about 'quiddity' and 'person'. See how he dismisses it by pointing to universal concepts.
4
Compare the two positions
The Ash'ari holds that existence is equivocal (verbal sharing), while the majority holds it is a single concept. Note how the majority further specifies that existence is a single reality differing by relations.
This is a key sectarian difference in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني في أن الوجود مفهوم مشترك بين الموجودات وهي زائدة على الماهيات (۱) ينبه على الأول الجزم بالوجود مع التردد في الخصوصية وصحة التقسيم إلى الواجب وغيره (۲) مع قطع النظر عن الوضع واللغة، فإن نوقض بالماهية والشخص قلنا مطلقهما أيضاً مشترك وتمام الحصر في الموجود والمعدوم، والقطع باتحاد مفهوم العدم، ولو بمعنى رفع الحقيقة إذ لا تغاير إلا بالاضافة) . المنقول عن الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري (۳) أن وجود كل شيء غير ذاته (٤) وليس للفظ الوجود مفهوم واحد مشترك بين الوجودات بل الاشتراك لفظي، والجمهور على أن له مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً بين الوجودات، إلا أنه عند المتكلمين حقيقة واحدة تختلف بالقيود والإضافات، حتى إن وجود الواجب هو كونه في الأعيان على ما يعقل من كون الإنسان (٤)
The second investigation is that existence is a single concept common among existents and it is additional to quiddities. It alerts to the first by the certainty of existence with hesitation about specificity and the validity of division into the Necessary and other, disregarding situation and language. If it is objected by quiddity and person, we say their universals are also common. And the complete enumeration is in existent and non-existent, and certainty of the unity of the concept of non-existence, even if by meaning of removal of reality, for there is no difference except by relation. And what is transmitted from Shaykh Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari is that the existence of each thing is other than its essence, and the word 'existence' does not have a single concept common among existents, rather the commonality is verbal. And the majority hold that it has a single concept common among existents, except that according to the theologians it is a single reality differing by restrictions and relations, such that the existence of the Necessary is its being in re in the way that 'human being' is conceived.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This is the second investigation: that existence is a single concept shared among existents and is additional to quiddities. The author provides two proofs: first, we can be certain of existence while being uncertain about its specific mode; second, the valid division of existence into the Necessary and other (possible) presupposes a common meaning. He then addresses a potential objection concerning 'quiddity' and 'person', responding that their universal meanings are also common. After affirming that the full enumeration is existence and non-existence, with non-existence having a single concept, he reports the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari: that existence is other than essence and that the term 'existence' is equivocal (verbal commonality). In contrast, the majority (including the author) hold that existence has one shared concept. For the theologians (mutakallimun), existence is a single reality that differs only by restrictions and relations, so the existence of the Necessary Existent is its being in re, analogous to how 'human being' is conceived as a universal.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that the word 'existence' has multiple unrelated meanings (equivocal), so it does not refer to a single shared concept.
Correction: The majority of scholars, including the author, argue that existence is a single concept common to all existents, and apparent differences are due to restrictions and relations.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Existence is a single common concept, not a homonym, supported by rational arguments and the majority view of theologians.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The source says non-existence has a single concept too. Pause and imagine your own non-existence. How does that real possibility change the way you value your next breath and the purpose you give your life?

The Philosophers' Objection and Initial Response
The author presents the philosophers' objection and initial response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection that existence cannot be superadded to the Necessary Being and the initial response to it.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue superaddition leads to absurdities; the response denies the claimed implications.

The philosophers raise a powerful objection: if existence were additional to the quiddity of the Necessary Being, it would entail logical contradictions. The author presents their argument and then offers a preliminary rebuttal, challenging the necessity of the consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the philosophers' claim
Read the first sentence to understand what the philosophers denied and why.
2
Examine each implication
The objection lists three consequences. Find them in the text.
The first is about being receptive and agent.
3
Study the response
The author responds to each implication. Note how the first is answered differently from the last two.
The response denies the necessary connection in the last two.
4
Reflect on the logic
Consider why priority by non-existence is possible, as the example of three and parity shows.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال : ومنعت الفلاسفة زيادته في الواجب إذ لو قام بماهيته لزم كونها قابلا وفاعلاً، وتقدمها بالوجود على الوجود ضرورة تقدم العلة على المعلول (وجواز) (۲) زوال الوجود عن الواجب ضرورة تقدم العلة نظراً إلى احتياجه في نفسه . وأجيب عن الأول بمنع بطلان اللازم وعن الأخيرين بمنع الملازمة، إذ التقدم قد لا يكون بالوجود كالثلاثة للفردية . وماهية الممكن لوجوده والمحتاج قد يمتنع زواله ضرورة كونه مقتضى الماهية . احتجت الفلاسفة على امتناع زيادة وجود الواجب على ماهيته بوجوده حاصلها أنه لو كان كذلك لزم محالات الأول كون الشيء قابلاً وفاعلاً (۳) وسيجيء بيان استحالته . الثاني (٤) : تقدم الشيء بوجوده على وجوده وهو ضروري الاستحالة، لا يحتاج إلى ما ذكره الإمام من أنه يفضي إلى وجود الشيء مرتين، وإلى التسلسل في الوجودات، لأن الوجود المتقدم إن كان نفس الماهية فذاك، وإلا عاد الكلام فيه وتسلسل . الثالث (٥) : إمكان زوال وجود الواجب (۱) وهو ضروري الاستحالة وجه اللزوم.
He said: The philosophers denied its superaddition in the Necessary, for if it were to subsist by its quiddity, it would entail it being receptive and agent, and its priority by existence over existence due to the necessity of the cause preceding the effect, and (the possibility of) the removal of existence from the Necessary due to the necessity of the cause preceding in view of its need in itself. And it was answered regarding the first by denying the invalidity of the necessary consequence, and regarding the last two by denying the correlation, for priority may not be by existence, like the priority of three to parity. And the quiddity of the possible is for its existence, and that which is in need may be impossible to be removed necessarily due to its being the necessary entailment of the quiddity. The philosophers argued for the impossibility of the superaddition of existence of the Necessary to its quiddity by its existence; the sum of it is that if it were so, impossibilities would follow: first, that a thing is both receptive and agent, and its impossibility will come later; second, the priority of a thing by its existence over its existence, which is necessarily impossible, and does not need what the Imam mentioned that it leads to a thing existing twice and to an infinite regress of existences, because if the prior existence is identical with the quiddity, then that is it, otherwise the same discourse applies and an infinite regress; third, the possibility of removal of existence from the Necessary, which is necessarily impossible. And the aspect of the necessary connection.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage discusses the philosophers' position that existence is not superadded (zā’id) to the quiddity of the Necessary Being (al-wājib). The author first summarizes their objection: if existence were superadded, three absurdities would follow: (1) the quiddity would be both receptive (qābil) and agent (fā‘il), implying a composition; (2) the quiddity would be prior by existence over existence (taqaddum bi-al-wujūd ‘alā al-wujūd), leading to an infinite regress; (3) existence could possibly be removed from the Necessary, contradicting its necessity. The author then gives the response to each: the first is denied by claiming that the impossibility of the consequent is not established; the last two are denied by denying the necessary correlation—priority may be essential (as three is prior to parity) not temporal or existential. The quiddity of possibles requires existence, but the Necessary's essence does not. The author then quotes the philosophers' own detailed proof, which again lists three impossibilities: (a) being receptive and agent, (b) priority of a thing by its existence over itself (absurd), and (c) possibility of removal of existence. The author criticizes the second point, saying it doesn't require the Imam's argument of double existence or regress; the simpler absurdity is self-priority. The third is also an absurdity. The structure shows the philosophers' culminating rationale.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib)
زيادة (ziyāda)
قابل (qābil)
فاعل (fā‘il)
تقدم (taqaddum)
علة (‘illa)
معلول (ma‘lūl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the philosophers' claim that existence could be removed from the Necessary?
A) By accepting the possibility.
B) By denying the correlation between need and possible removal.
C) By asserting that the Necessary does not need existence.
D) By stating that removal would only occur if existence were separate.

Key Takeaway
The initial response denies the claimed absurdities by challenging the necessary connection between superaddition and the supposed impossibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the philosophers' objection, did any doubt creep into your heart? The response denies the correlation, but does your faith rest on intellectual arguments or on personal certainty? Are you willing to study these debates until your belief in Allah's attributes is built on a solid foundation, not just inherited? This week, pick one attribute of Allah and write down any logical difficulties you have, then seek answers from the scholars.
?
The philosophers argued that superaddition leads to absurdities. In your own life, do you avoid asking hard questions about your faith because you fear the answers? The response shows that careful reasoning can defend the truth. What is one question about Allah's existence or attributes that you have hesitated to explore? Commit to finding a reliable source to study it this month.
?
The text uses subtle distinctions like 'priority by existence' vs 'priority by essence'. These may seem academic, but they affect how you conceive of God. Do you think of Allah as having attributes that are 'added' to His essence, or as being simple? How does your mental picture of God align with what the scholars teach? Will you spend time in reflection (tafakkur) to purify your understanding of the Divine?

The Philosophers' Detailed Proof of Three Impossibilities
The philosophers present their detailed proof of three impossibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' detailed proof that superaddition of existence leads to three impossibilities, and the reasoning behind each.

Core Idea
The philosophers' proof relies on three logical impossibilities: being both agent and patient, self-priority, and potential removal.

After the initial exchange, the philosophers elaborate their proof that superaddition of existence is impossible for the Necessary Being. They list three distinct logical impossibilities that would follow, each explained in detail.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' proof
Start with 'احتجت الفلاسفة' and identify the three impossibilities they present.
They are numbered: الأول, الثاني, الثالث.
2
Understand the first impossibility
The quiddity being both receptive and agent – note that the author says its impossibility will be explained later.
This implies composition.
3
Analyze the second impossibility
The priority of a thing by its existence over its existence – the author explains it is self-evidently impossible, and criticizes the Imam's argument of double existence and regress.
The simpler absurdity is self-priority.
4
Examine the third impossibility
The possibility of removal of existence from the Necessary – the author says it is necessarily impossible.
5
Reflect on the reasoning
Consider how each impossibility is derived from the assumption of superaddition.

He said: The philosophers denied its superaddition in the Necessary, for if it were to subsist by its quiddity, it would entail it being receptive and agent, and its priority by existence over existence due to the necessity of the cause preceding the effect, and (the possibility of) the removal of existence from the Necessary due to the necessity of the cause preceding in view of its need in itself. And it was answered regarding the first by denying the invalidity of the necessary consequence, and regarding the last two by denying the correlation, for priority may not be by existence, like the priority of three to parity. And the quiddity of the possible is for its existence, and that which is in need may be impossible to be removed necessarily due to its being the necessary entailment of the quiddity. The philosophers argued for the impossibility of the superaddition of existence of the Necessary to its quiddity by its existence; the sum of it is that if it were so, impossibilities would follow: first, that a thing is both receptive and agent, and its impossibility will come later; second, the priority of a thing by its existence over its existence, which is necessarily impossible, and does not need what the Imam mentioned that it leads to a thing existing twice and to an infinite regress of existences, because if the prior existence is identical with the quiddity, then that is it, otherwise the same discourse applies and an infinite regress; third, the possibility of removal of existence from the Necessary, which is necessarily impossible. And the aspect of the necessary connection.

Commentary
This passage discusses the philosophers' position that existence is not superadded (zā’id) to the quiddity of the Necessary Being (al-wājib). The author first summarizes their objection: if existence were superadded, three absurdities would follow: (1) the quiddity would be both receptive (qābil) and agent (fā‘il), implying a composition; (2) the quiddity would be prior by existence over existence (taqaddum bi-al-wujūd ‘alā al-wujūd), leading to an infinite regress; (3) existence could possibly be removed from the Necessary, contradicting its necessity. The author then gives the response to each: the first is denied by claiming that the impossibility of the consequent is not established; the last two are denied by denying the necessary correlation—priority may be essential (as three is prior to parity) not temporal or existential. The quiddity of possibles requires existence, but the Necessary's essence does not. The author then quotes the philosophers' own detailed proof, which again lists three impossibilities: (a) being receptive and agent, (b) priority of a thing by its existence over itself (absurd), and (c) possibility of removal of existence. The author criticizes the second point, saying it doesn't require the Imam's argument of double existence or regress; the simpler absurdity is self-priority. The third is also an absurdity. The structure shows the philosophers' culminating rationale.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib)
زيادة (ziyāda)
قابل (qābil)
فاعل (fā‘il)
تقدم (taqaddum)
تسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the second impossibility?
A) It requires the Imam's argument of double existence.
B) It is necessarily impossible and does not need elaborate proof.
C) It is not actually an impossibility.
D) It leads to an infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' proof centers on three self-evident impossibilities that would arise if existence were superadded to the Necessary Being's quiddity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers prove that for the Necessary Being, existence cannot be added to its essence. This means He is existence itself. Does your own existence feel like something added to you, or do you sense that you are your existence? How does that comparison expose your complete dependence on Him for every moment?
?
The second impossibility shows that if existence were superadded, the quiddity would have to exist before its own existence. Pause and consider: Have you ever tried to base your identity on something that must come after you—like achievements, possessions, or relationships? What would it mean to let go of those and rest solely in the One whose existence is His essence?
?
The third impossibility states that the Necessary Being's existence cannot be removed. But your existence can be removed at any moment. Tonight, before you sleep, say to yourself: 'I am possible; He is necessary. My existence is a gift that can be withdrawn.' How should that truth change your priorities for tomorrow?

The Philosophers' Denial of Additional Existence in God
Taftazani summarizes the philosophers' position and their three arguments, with a preliminary response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that existence is not additional to God's essence and the initial rebuttal of their argument.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that if existence were added to God, His essence would be both recipient and agent—a combination they deem impossible.

Taftazani begins by summarizing the philosophers' denial of the addition of existence to God's essence. They present three arguments, but even in this summary, a brief response is given. This lesson establishes the foundation for the detailed discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation to get the overall sense of the philosophers' claim and the initial answer.
Notice that the passage is a summary; each point will be expanded later.
2
Identify the three impossibilities
The philosophers claim that if existence were additional, three impossibilities would follow: (1) essence being both recipient and agent, (2) priority of essence by existence over existence, (3) possible cessation of God's existence. Find these in the text.
3
Note the initial answer
The author records an initial response: for the first, they deny that the consequent is impossible; for the latter two, they deny the entailment (luzum). Highlight this in the translation.
The example of threeness and oddness illustrates that priority can be by essence, not by existence.

وماهية الممكن لوجوده والمحتاج قد يمتنع زواله ضرورة كونه مقتضى الماهية) .

وأما الثالث: فلأن الوجود إذا كان محتاجاً إلى غيره كان ممكناً وكان جائز الزوال نظراً إلى ذاته، وإلا لكان واجباً لذاته، هذا خلف. وإنما قلنا نظراً إلى ذاته دفعاً لما قيل : لا نسلم أن كل ممكن جائز الزوال، وإنما يكون كذلك لولم يكن واجباً بالغير ) وأجيب عن الأول : بأنا لا نسلم استحالة كون الشيء قابلاً وفاعلاً، وسيجيء الكلام على دليلها .
وعن الثاني : بأنا لا نسلم لزوم تقدم الماهية على الوجود بالوجود، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو لزم تقدم العلة على المعلول بالوجود وهو ممنوع، ودعوى الضرورة غير مسموعة ، وإنما الضرورة تقدمها بما علة به إن كانت بالوجود، فبالوجود أو بالماهية فبالماهية كما في اللوازم المستندة إلى نفس الماهية، فإن الماهية تتقدمها بذاتها ومن حيث كونها تلك الماهية من غير اعتبار وجودها أو عدمها، كالثلاثة للفردية ، وذلك كالقابل فإن تقدمه على المقبول ضروري، لكنه قد
كالأربعة للزوجية.
He said: The philosophers denied the addition of existence to the Necessary Being, for if existence were subsisting by His essence, then that essence would be both recipient and agent, and priority of the essence by existence over existence [would follow] due to the necessary priority of the cause over the effect, and the permissibility of the cessation of existence from the Necessary Being due to the necessary priority of the cause, considering its need in itself. And it was answered regarding the first by denying the invalidity of the consequent, and regarding the latter two by denying the entailment, for priority may not be by existence, like threeness for oddness. And the essence of the possible is for its existence, and the needy may be such that its cessation is impossible due to its being a necessary consequence of the essence. The philosophers argued for the impossibility of the addition of the existence of the Necessary Being to His essence by [the following] summary: if it were so, then impossibilities would follow: first, a thing being both recipient and agent, and its impossibility will be explained later. Second: priority of a thing by its existence over its existence, which is necessarily impossible, and it does not need what the Imam mentioned that it leads to a thing existing twice and to an infinite regress of existences, because if the prior existence is the essence itself, then that is it, otherwise the same argument recurs and regresses. Third: possibility of cessation of the existence of the Necessary Being, which is necessarily impossible. The manner of entailment: As for the first: because the essence would be a recipient of existence from the perspective of being supposed, and an agent from the perspective of necessitating. As for the second: because existence would then need the essence as an accident needs its substrate, so it would be possible due to its need for another, thus it would depend on a cause which is the essence alone, since it is impossible for the existence of the Necessary Being to depend on another, and every cause is necessarily prior to its effect, so the essence would be prior by existence over existence. As for the third: because if existence is in need of another, it would be possible and permissible of cessation considering itself, otherwise it would be necessary by itself, which is contrary to the assumption. And we said 'considering itself' to rebut what was said: we do not accept that every possible is permissible of cessation; rather, it is only so if it is not necessary by another. And it was answered regarding the first: we do not accept the impossibility of a thing being both recipient and agent, and the discussion of its proof will come later. Regarding the second: we do not accept the necessary priority of the essence over existence by existence; rather, that would follow only if the priority of cause over effect were by existence, and that is denied, and the claim of necessity is not admitted. Rather, the necessary priority is by that by which it is a cause: if by existence, then by existence; or by essence, then by essence—as in necessary concomitants dependent on the essence itself, for the essence is prior to them by its very self and by virtue of being that essence, without consideration of its existence or non-existence, like threeness for oddness. And that is like the recipient, for its priority over the received is necessary, but it is ... like fourness for evenness.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalam argument: the philosophers' proof that existence is not additional to God's essence, followed by the refutation. The author begins by stating the philosophers' denial. They argue: if existence were an additional attribute subsisting in God's essence, then that essence would have to be both a recipient (qabil) of existence and an agent (fa'il) causing it—which they deem impossible. Moreover, the essence would be prior to existence by existence, since a cause must be prior to its effect, implying a vicious regress. Also, since existence depends on the essence, it would be possible for it to cease, which is impossible for the Necessary Being. The author then notes a brief preliminary answer. After this, the full argument is given as three distinct impossibilities: (1) the essence being both recipient and agent, (2) priority of a thing by its existence over its own existence, and (3) the possibility of cessation of God's existence. Each is explained in detail. Then the refutation is given: For the first, the Ash'arite position is that being both recipient and agent is not impossible—this will be argued later. For the second, the entailment of priority by existence is denied. The cause may be prior by essence, not by existence, as in the case of threeness being prior to oddness: the essence of three entails oddness without temporal priority. Thus, the essence of God can be prior to existence in terms of essence, not time. For the third, they deny that every possible is liable to cessation; a possible that is necessary by another (i.e., caused to exist perpetually) does not cease. Hence, the philosophers' proof is refuted.

The philosophers' three arguments against the addition of existence to God's essence are based on the perceived impossibility of the essence being both recipient and agent, priority by existence, and possible cessation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that God's existence is not an addition to His essence but is His very reality, how must your understanding of His oneness (tawhid) change? What aspect of your worship might currently treat Him as if He has parts? Commit to one change today.
?
The philosophers argued that God cannot be both recipient and agent. In your own life, where do you unconsciously assign roles to God that limit His absolute agency? For example, do you ever think 'God can't help me with this' as if He were a passive recipient of your circumstances? Repent and realign your trust.
?
The passage teaches that dependence on the essence does not imply possible cessation. Are there any beliefs or habits you hold that imply God's attributes could cease (like doubting His mercy when you sin)? How will you anchor your heart in the truth that His necessary attributes are eternal and unchanging?

The Three Philosophers' Arguments Explained
Taftazani expounds the three arguments of the philosophers with detailed reasoning for each impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the detailed reasoning behind each of the three impossibilities the philosophers claim follow from the addition of existence to God's essence.

Core Idea
The philosophers argue that if existence were additional, the essence would be prior to existence, causing a vicious regress, and existence would be contingent and liable to cessation.

After summarizing the philosophers' position, Taftazani now presents the full argument with the entailment of each impossibility. This lesson elaborates on why the philosophers consider each consequence impossible, using logical reasoning based on causality and the nature of existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage (the summary and the explanation of entailments) and its translation. Note that the passage begins with the summary of the three arguments and then explains each.
2
Examine each impossibility in detail
For each of the three impossibilities, identify the logical steps the philosophers take. The first is about being both recipient and agent; the second about priority by existence; the third about possible cessation.
The second argument includes a discussion of infinite regress and the Imam's objection.
3
Understand the philosophers' reasoning for the third argument
The third argument involves a distinction: existence considered in itself is possible of cessation, but the philosophers preempt the objection that some possibles are necessary by another by clarifying that they consider existence in itself.
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalam argument: the philosophers' proof that existence is not additional to God's essence, followed by the refutation. Refutation of the Philosophers' Arguments
Taftazani refutes each of the three philosophers' arguments using Ash'arite principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite refutation of each of the three philosophers' arguments, particularly the distinction between priority by essence and priority by existence.

Core Idea
The Ash'arite response denies the impossibility of being both recipient and agent, and refutes the need for priority by existence by showing that priority can be by essence alone.

After presenting the philosophers' arguments in full, Taftazani now details the refutation. For each argument, he provides a response grounded in the principles of kalam. The key move is distinguishing between priority by existence and priority by essence, using examples like threeness being prior to oddness by essence, not by time.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic refutations and their translation. Notice that the refutation addresses each philosophers' argument in order.
The refutation of the first argument is brief; the second is explained in detail with examples.
2
Analyze the refutation of the second argument
The Ash'arites deny that priority of cause over effect must be by existence. They argue it can be by essence, like threeness being prior to oddness. Understand this crucial distinction.
This refutation undermines the second and third arguments because if existence is a necessary concomitant, it cannot cease and does not require existential priority.
3
Apply the refutation to the third argument
Although the text is truncated, the logic implies that if existence is a necessary concomitant of the essence (like oddness for three), it cannot cease. The example of fourness and evenness reinforces this.
The third argument is refuted because existence as a necessary concomitant does not cease.
