First Reductio Argument on Existence and Essence
Al-Taftazani presents the first reductio proof that existence is not additional to essence for the Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first reductio proof that existence is not additional to the essence of the Necessary Being, and how it refutes the theory that existence is a separate attribute.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's existence is a special existence, not a separate attribute.

Al-Taftazani begins his proof that existence is not additional to the essence of the Necessary Being with a powerful reductio argument. He assumes the opposite—that existence is a separate attribute—and shows it leads to absurdities. This lesson walks through the logical steps of that reductio, including the objection and its final answer.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reductio premise
Start by grasping the assumption: suppose the existence of the Necessary Being were devoid of any accompanying essence (i.e., existence is a separate attribute). This sets up the reductio.
The term 'devoid' here means existence without an essence attached, not nonexistence.
2
Follow the first branch (due to essence)
If the devoidness is due to His essence, then every existence would be similarly devoid, because an essence's necessary entailment cannot fail. But we know contingent beings are not devoid, so this is false. Moreover, all existences would be necessary, leading to multiple necessary beings.
3
Follow the second branch (due to other)
If the devoidness is due to something else, then the Necessary Being would need that other for His necessity, contradicting self-sufficiency.
4
Consider the objection and reply
An objector suggests that devoidness is simply the absence of what necessitates accompaniment, so no external cause is needed. The reply is that even that absence would be needed, still implying dependence.
5
Grasp the final answer
The final answer is that the Necessary Being's existence is a special existence (wujud khas) distinct in reality from all other existences, so the reductio's assumption does not apply.
This special existence means existence and essence are identical for the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : لو كان وجود الواجب مجرداً عن مقارنة الماهية فحصول هذا الوصف له إن كان لذاته لزم أن يكون كل وجود كذلك لامتناع تخلف مقتضى الذات، وقد مر بطلانه بل واجباً فيلزم تعدد الواجب، وإن كان لغيره لزم احتياج الواجب في وجوبه إلى الغير ضرورة توقف وجوبه على التجرد المتوقف على ذلك الغير لا يقال : يكفي في التجرد عدم ما يقتضي المقارنة لأنا نقول: فيحتاج إلى ذلك العدم. أجيب بأنه لذاته يقتضي هذا الوجود الخاص المخالف بالحقيقة لسائر الموجودات .
First: If the existence of the Necessary Being were devoid of accompanying essence, then the occurrence of this description to Him, if it is due to His essence, then every existence would be like that, due to the impossibility of the consequent of the essence being left behind, and its falsehood has already passed, rather it would be necessary, thus necessitating multiplicity of the necessary being; and if it is due to something else, then the Necessary Being would need something else in its necessity, by necessity of its necessity being dependent on the devoidness which depends on that other. It cannot be said: Sufficient for devoidness is the absence of what necessitates accompaniment, for we say: then He would need that absence. It is answered: By His essence He necessitates this special existence which is different in reality from all other existences.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the first of five arguments demonstrating that existence is not additional to the essence of the Necessary Being. The argument is a conditional proof: Suppose the existence of the Necessary Being were devoid of any accompanying essence (i.e., existence is a separate attribute). Then the occurrence of this description—being devoid—to Him either is due to His essence or due to something else. If due to His essence, then every existence would be similarly devoid, because an essence's necessary entailment cannot be left behind. But we already know that is false (contingent existences are not devoid). Moreover, it would make every existence necessary, implying multiple necessary beings. If the devoidness is due to something else, then the Necessary Being would need that other for His necessity, contradicting self-sufficiency. The objector suggests that devoidness is simply the absence of what necessitates accompaniment, so no further cause is needed. The answer is that even that absence would be needed, still implying dependence. The final answer is that the Necessary Being's existence is a special existence (wujud khas) distinct in reality from all other existences, so the argument against separation fails.

Key Terms
وجود
ماهية
واجب
تجرد
وجود خاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Devoidness means non-existence of existence.
Correction: Devoidness here means existence without an accompanying essence, i.e., existence is a separate attribute, not non-existence.

Checkpoint
In the first argument, if the devoidness of existence from essence were due to the essence itself, what would follow?
A) The Necessary Being would need something else.
B) Every existence would be devoid, leading to multiple necessary beings.
C) Existence would be impossible.
D) The essence would be non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The first reductio shows that assuming existence is separate from essence for the Necessary Being leads to logical absurdities, affirming the doctrine of the special existence (wujud khas).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever think of God's existence as something He 'has' rather than what He is? How does this affect your worship and reliance on Him?
?
If God's existence were separate from His essence, what would that imply about your own dependence on Him? Could you truly rely on a being whose existence is not identical with its essence?
?
This lesson argues that God's existence is unique and different from all else. How does that truth change the way you relate to the world around you and to your own sense of existence?

Why the Necessary Being Must Have a Special Existence
Al-Taftazani argues that if existence were a common nature, the Necessary Being could not be the principle of contingent things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Necessary Being must have a special existence (wujud khas) rather than a generic or abstract existence, based on the argument that only a unique existence can be the principle of contingent things.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being's existence is unique and not a shared nature.

Al-Taftazani presents a second argument to prove that existence is not an accident added to essence. He starts from the obvious fact that the Necessary Being is the source (mabda') of all contingent things. If His existence were a mere abstract nature, logical absurdities would follow. This lesson walks through the argument's steps and reveals why the Necessary Being must have a special, unique existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the logical connectors like 'if', 'then', 'otherwise'.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the three hypotheticals
The passage examines three ways the Necessary Being's existence could be a principle: (1) by essence, (2) as existence with condition of devoidness, (3) as existence conditioned by devoidness. Note the absurdity that follows each.
The third is a bit tricky: it distinguishes between essential impossibility and conditional impossibility.
3
Understand the final response
The answer states that the Necessary Being's essence is a special existence (wujud khas) distinct from all others. This avoids the absurdities because the principle status is unique to that essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : الواجب مبدأ الممكنات فلو كان وجوداً مجرداً فكونه مبدا للممكنات إن كان لذاته فيلزم أن يكون كل وجود كذلك، محال لاستحالة وهو كون وجود زيد علة لنفسه ولعلله، وإلا فإن كان هو الوجود مع قيد التجرد لزم تركب المبدأ بل عدمه ضرورة إذ أحد جزأيه وهو التجرد عدمي، وإن كان بشرط التجرد لزم جواز كون كل وجود مبدأ لكل وجود، إلا أن الحكم تخلف عنه لانتفاء شرط المبدئية. ومعلوم أن كون الشيء مبدأ لنفسه ولعلله ممتنع بالذات، لا بواسطة انتفاء شرط المبدئية . والجواب : أن ذلك لذاته الذي هو وجود خاص مباين لسائر الوجودات فلا يلزم أن يكون كل وجود كذلك .
Second: The Necessary Being is the principle of contingent things. So if He were a mere existence, then His being the principle of contingent things, if it is due to His essence, then every existence would be like that, which is impossible because of the impossibility of the existence of Zayd being a cause for itself and for its cause. Otherwise, if it is existence with the condition of devoidness, then there would be composition of the principle, rather its non-existence, by necessity, since one of its two parts, which is devoidness, is non-existent; and if it is conditioned by devoidness, then it would be possible for every existence to be a principle for every existence, except that the judgment would be left behind due to the absence of the condition of principality. And it is known that a thing being a principle for itself and for its cause is impossible in itself, not by means of the absence of the condition of principality. And the answer: That is by His essence, which is a special existence distinct from all other existences, so it does not necessitate that every existence be like that.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument that existence is not additional to essence. It starts from the fact that the Necessary Being is the principle (mabda') of all contingent things. If His existence were a mere shared nature (wujud mujarrad), then His being a principle must be either by His essence or by something else. If by His essence, every existence would be a principle, which is absurd because then Zayd's existence would be a cause for itself and for its own cause (since it is contingent and needs a cause). If it is existence with the condition of devoidness (tajarrad), then the principle would be composite, and one part (devoidness) is non-existent, so the principle itself would be non-existent. If it is conditioned by devoidness (i.e., a condition external to the essence), then every existence would potentially be a principle, but the actual judgment of being a principle would be absent due to the missing condition—but this is not the real impossibility; the real impossibility is that a thing cannot be a principle for itself and its cause by essence. Thus, the existence of the Necessary Being must be special (wujud khas) and not a common nature, so the separation of existence from essence is avoided.

Key Terms
مبدأ
وجود
تجرد
علة
وجود خاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of a thing being a principle for itself is due to a missing condition (shart).
Correction: The passage states that such impossibility is essential (bi al-dhat), not by means of the absence of a condition. It is inherently impossible for anything to be a cause of itself and its own cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion of the second argument regarding the existence of the Necessary Being?
A) Existence is a generic nature for all beings.
B) The Necessary Being's existence is special and not shared.
C) The Necessary Being cannot be a principle.
D) Devoidness is a positive attribute.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being must have a special existence distinct from all other existences for it to serve as the unique principle of contingent things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's existence were like any other existence, He could not be the source of all things. Do you live as if His existence is truly unlike anything else? In what areas of life do you unconsciously equate Him with created causes?
?
The argument shows that the impossibility of a thing causing itself is essential—not due to a missing condition. Do you sometimes assume that Allah's power is limited by conditions? What would change if you saw His power as absolute and not subject to the same limitations as created things?
?
The Necessary Being's existence is completely distinct from yours. How does that distinction shape your worship? Do you approach Him as the utterly unique One, or do you treat Him as a bigger version of created beings? Commit to one specific way you will remind yourself of His uniqueness this week.

Third Argument: Participation and Difference
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that participation in existence implies a difference between existence and essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument against the extramental existence of necessary existence and Al-Taftazani's refutation by distinguishing absolute existence from special existence.

Core Idea
Participation in absolute existence does not make existence an extra attribute.

This lesson presents a specific objection: if God shares existence with contingent things, then His existence must be distinct from His essence. Al-Taftazani's response hinges on the difference between absolute existence, a mental concept, and special existence, which is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being. Understanding this argument clarifies a key distinction in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: premise then conclusion.
2
Identify the problem
The argument tries to prove that existence is additional to essence by using participation and difference.
3
Examine the answer
Al-Taftazani distinguishes between absolute existence (shared) and special existence (unique to each thing).
4
Reflect on the refutation
Understand why the answer invalidates the argument: the dispute is about special existence, not absolute existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : الواجب يشارك الممكنات في الوجود ويخالفها في الحقيقة، وما به المشاركة غير ما به المخالفة، فيكون وجوده مغايراً لحقيقته والجواب : أن ما به المشاركة هو الوجود المطلق، والحقيقة هو الوجود الخاص، وهو المتنازع فيه
Third: The Necessary Being shares with contingent things in existence and differs from them in reality. That by which He shares is not that by which He differs, so His existence would be other than His reality. And the answer: That by which He shares is absolute existence, and the reality is the special existence, which is the point of dispute.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument and its answer. The argument is: The Necessary Being shares with contingent things in the attribute of existence, but differs from them in reality (haqiqa). Since that by which He shares (existence) is different from that by which He differs (reality), it follows that His existence is other than His reality. This would support the view that existence is additional to essence. The answer is that what they share is 'absolute existence' (al-wujud al-mutlaq), which is a mental concept, while the reality of the Necessary Being is 'special existence' (al-wujud al-khas), which is identical to His essence. The dispute is precisely about this special existence, not about absolute existence. Thus, the argument fails because it conflates the two senses of existence.

Misconception: Participation in existence proves existence is an attribute added to essence.
Correction: Participation is only in absolute existence (mental concept), not in concrete special existence.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the third argument, what is the point of dispute?
A) Whether the Necessary Being exists.
B) Whether absolute existence is additional to essence.
C) Whether special existence is additional to essence.
D) Whether contingent things share existence with God.

Key Takeaway
The third argument fails because it mistakenly uses absolute existence to argue about special existence.

The Fourth Argument on Existence and the Necessary Being
Al-Taftazani exhaustively examines possibilities for the relationship between the Necessary Being and existence, then provides the answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument concerning whether the Necessary Being's essence is identical to existence or something additional, and how the theologian resolves the dilemma.

Core Idea
The Necessary Being is the special existence itself, distinct from all other existences.

This lesson continues the investigation into the nature of existence. Al-Taftazani presents a sophisticated argument that exhaustively considers the possible relationships between the Necessary Being and existence. By examining these possibilities, he shows that only one understanding avoids absurdity: that the Necessary Being is the special existence itself. The opponent's objection and the answer reveal a crucial distinction between absolute existence and special existence.

Guided Path
1
Understand the starting point
The argument begins by assuming the opponent's view that existence is what? Read the first conditional: if the Necessary Being is identical to absolute existence, then every existing thing would be necessary. Why?
This shows the first absurdity.
2
Examine the second and third possibilities
If existence is with the condition of devoidness, or conditioned by devoidness, what problems arise? The author points out composition or dependence.
Devoidness (tajarrad) is non-existent, so can't be a part.
3
Explore the fourth case
If existence is other than the essence, then it must be either without external existence (impossible) or with it. The latter splits into inclusion (also impossible) or addition. This last is what the opponent wants.
This sets up the opponent's desired conclusion.
4
Study the answer
The theologian responds by distinguishing between absolute existence and special existence. The Necessary Being is the special existence itself, not absolute existence. Thus, the addition of absolute existence in the mind is conceded.
The key distinction resolves the dilemma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : الواجب إن كان نفس الكون في الأعيان - أعني الوجود المطلق لزم تعدد الواجب ضرورة أن وجود زيد غير وجود عمرو، وإن كان هو الكون مع القيد التجرد لزم تركب الواجب من الوجود والتجرد مع أنه عدمي لا يصلح جزءاً للواجب، أو بشرط التجرد لزم أن يكون الواجب واجباً لذاته، بل بشرطه الذي هو التجرد، وإن كان غير الكون في الأعيان فإن كان بدون الكون في الأعيان فمحال ضرورة أنه لا يعقل الوجود بدون الكون، وإن كان مع الكون فإما أن يكون الكون داخلا فيه وهو محال ضرورة امتناع تركب الواجب، أو خارجاً عنه وهو المطلوب، لأن معناه زيادة الوجود على ما هو حقيقة الواجب . والجواب : أنه نفس الكون الخاص المجرد المخالف الأكوان، ولا نزاع في زيادة الكون المطلق عليه .
Fourth: The Necessary Being, if He is the very existence in external things — I mean absolute existence — then multiplicity of the necessary being would be necessitated, by necessity, because the existence of Zayd is other than the existence of Amr. And if He is existence with the condition of devoidness, then composition of the Necessary Being from existence and devoidness would be necessitated, while it is non-existent and not fit to be a part of the Necessary Being; or if conditioned by devoidness, then the Necessary Being would be necessary by His essence, but by His condition which is devoidness. And if He is other than existence in external things, then if He is without existence in external things, it is impossible, by necessity, because existence is not conceivable without being in external things; and if He is with existence, then either existence is included in Him, which is impossible by necessity due to the impossibility of composition of the Necessary Being, or it is outside Him, and that is what is required, because it means the addition of existence to what is the reality of the Necessary Being. And the answer: He is the special existence itself, the devoid one, different from existences, and there is no dispute about the addition of absolute existence to Him.
Source pages p. 317

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, which exhaustively examines possibilities for the relationship between the Necessary Being and existence. It begins by considering if the Necessary Being is identical to absolute existence (al-kawn fi al-a'yan), then every existing thing would be necessary, leading to multiple necessary beings. If He is existence with the condition of devoidness (tajarrad), this would make Him composite, and devoidness is non-existent, so composition from a non-existent part is impossible. If He is existence conditioned by devoidness (as an external condition), then His necessity would depend on that condition, which is unacceptable. If He is other than existence in external things, then two subcases: either He is without any external existence, which is impossible since existence is only conceivable as external; or He is with external existence. In the latter case, either existence is included in Him (making Him composite) or outside Him (meaning existence is added to His essence). The opponent desires the latter to prove addition. The answer is that the Necessary Being is the very special existence (al-kawn al-khas al-mujarrad), which is distinct from all other existences. There is no dispute that absolute existence is added to Him in the mind, but special existence is identical to His essence.

Key Terms

تجرد
وجود خاص
تركب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition (shart) is part of the essence of the Necessary Being.
Correction: The condition is external; the theologian clarifies that devoidness is non-existent and cannot be a part, and the condition cannot be a condition for the essence.

Checkpoint
What is the key point of the answer to the fourth argument?
A) The Necessary Being is identical to absolute existence.
B) The Necessary Being is composite.
C) The Necessary Being is the special existence itself, distinct from others.
D) Existence is added to the essence of the Necessary Being.

Key Takeaway
The Necessary Being is the special existence itself, distinct from all contingent existences, so absolute existence is only conceptually added.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you think of Allah's existence, do you unconsciously picture it as similar to other existences, like a 'big being' among beings? ?
The argument rejects composition in the Necessary Being. Are there any 'parts' in your understanding of Allah—like mercy separate from justice, or will separate from power—that you treat as if they are added to His essence? How can you train yourself to see His attributes as one undivided reality? Commit to reciting the verse 'ليس كمثله شيء' (Nothing is like Him) with full reflection before each prayer for the next seven days.

Fifth Argument: Existence Known vs. Essence Unknown
Al-Taftazani presents and refutes the fifth argument for existence as additional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fifth argument fails to prove that existence is additional to God's essence, and how the distinction between absolute and special existence resolves it.

Core Idea
The fifth argument confuses absolute existence with God's own special existence.

The fifth argument tries to prove that existence is other than essence by appealing to what we know. We know existence necessarily, but God's reality is unknown — so they must differ. But is that really sound? Al-Taftazani presents the answer that distinguishes between two types of existence, thereby showing the argument doesn't touch the real issue. Let's unpack it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the structure: first the argument, then the answer, then the author's summary.
2
Identify the argument's structure
Identify the premises: existence is known necessarily; the reality of the Necessary Being is unknown by consensus; the unknown is different from the known. Conclusion: existence is other than essence.
This is a syllogism: known ≠ unknown, so existence ≠ essence.
3
Understand the answer's distinction
The answer says the known existence is 'absolute existence' (al-wujud al-mutlaq), not the 'special existence' (al-wujud al-khas) which is identical to essence. The argument only works if the known existence is special, but it's not.
Absolute existence is a mental concept shared by all existents; special existence is unique to each being.
4
Connect to the broader debate
The author notes that everyone agrees absolute existence is mentally additional to essence. The real dispute is about special existence. The five arguments fail to prove that special existence is additional.
This passage is part of a series refuting arguments for ziyada.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : الوجود معلوم بالضرورة، وحقيقة الواجب غير معلومة اتفاقاً وغير المعلوم هو غير المعلوم ضرورة والجواب : أن المعلوم هو الوجود المطلق للمغاير للخاص الذي هو نفس الحقيقة، وإلى هذه الأجوبة أشار بقوله : لا نزاع في زيادة الوجود المطلق أي على ماهية الواجب وإنما النزاع في زيادة وجوده الخاص، وما ذكر من الوجوه لا يدل عليها .
Fifth: Existence is known necessarily, and the reality of the Necessary Being is unknown unanimously, and the unknown is other than the known necessarily. And the answer: That which is known is the absolute existence of that which is other than the special existence which is the reality itself. And to these answers he alludes by his saying: There is no dispute about the addition of absolute existence to the essence of the Necessary Being; rather the dispute is about the addition of His special existence. And what has been mentioned of the arguments do not indicate it.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, which argues from common knowledge: Existence is known to us by necessity (e.g., we know that things exist), but the reality (haqiqa) of the Necessary Being is unknown by consensus (the human mind cannot grasp God's essence). Since the known (existence) and the unknown (essence) are different, it follows that existence is other than essence. The answer distinguishes between two types of existence: absolute existence (al-wujud al-mutlaq), which is known and is a mental concept shared by all existents, and special existence (al-wujud al-khas), which is identical to the essence of the Necessary Being and is unknown in its reality. Therefore, what we know is the absolute existence, not the special existence, so the argument does not prove that existence is additional to essence. The passage concludes with the author's summary: the earlier five arguments have been refuted by distinguishing between absolute existence (which is agreed to be additional in mental conception) and special existence (which is the point of dispute).

Key Terms
معلوم بالضرورة
حقيقة الواجب
وجود مطلق
وجود خاص
زيادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The existence we know in the argument is God's own existence.
Correction: The existence we know is absolute existence, a mental concept, not God's special existence which is His essence.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction in the answer to the fifth argument?
A) Between necessary and contingent existence.
B) Between mental and external existence.
C) Between absolute existence and special existence.
D) Between known and unknown attributes.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument fails because it confuses absolute existence, a universal mental concept, with special existence, which is identical to God's essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Does your everyday faith treat God as a known theoretical concept or as the profoundly mysterious, intimate reality that He is? If you cannot grasp His essence, what does that mean for how you relate to Him in prayer and trust?
?
The fifth argument mistakenly reduces God's existence to a universal idea. In your life, when do you reduce God to a concept—something you know about but don't live by? What would it take to live in the reality of His special existence, which is beyond your full understanding yet more certain than anything else?

Existence is Not a Generic Nature
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that existence is a univocal genus with identical concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that existence is a univocal genus and explains that existence is predicated as a non-essential concomitant.

Core Idea
Existence is not a genus; it is analogical, and its absolute predication is non-essential.

In this passage, Al-Taftazani addresses a key objection: if existence were a generic nature, then all existents would share the same properties. He denies this and uses the analogy of lights to show that existences are similar in reality yet differ in their specific concomitants.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Carefully read both the objection (the claim that existence is a generic nature) and the answer (Al-Taftazani's denial and explanation).
Notice the structure: 'If it is said... I say...'
2
Understand the analogy of lights
Al-Taftazani compares existences to lights: different lights (sun, fire) share the nature of light but have different properties. This refutes the idea that a shared nature forces identical properties.
3
Learn the concept of non-essential concomitant
The absolute term 'existence' is predicated of all existents as a non-essential concomitant (lazim ghayr dhati), not as a genus. This means it accompanies existence but is not part of the essence.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
This answer preserves the uniqueness of the Necessary Being's existence while admitting that existence is a common term. The properties of necessity, being a principle, etc., are not shared by contingent beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(قال) : فإن قيل : الوجود طبيعة نوعية فلا تختلف لوازمها . قلت : ممنوع بل الوجودات متحالفة بالحقيقة يجب للبعض منها ما يمتنع على البعض كالأنوار ويقع المطلق عليها وقوع لازم غير ذاتي لها .
(He said): For if it is said: Existence is a generic nature, so its concomitants do not differ. I say: It is denied; rather existences are similar in reality; some of them have what is impossible for others, like lights; and the absolute is predicated of them as a non-essential concomitant.
Source pages p. 318

Commentary
This passage presents a final objection and answer. The objector claims that existence is a generic nature (tabi'a naw'iyya) that applies equally to all existents, so its concomitants (lawazim) should be the same for all. This would mean that the properties of the Necessary Being's existence (e.g., necessity, being a principle) would also apply to contingent existences, which is false. Therefore, the existence of the Necessary Being must be different in kind. The answer is that the premise is denied: existence is not a generic nature that is univocal. Rather, existences (wujudat) are similar in reality (mutahalifa bi al-haqiqa) but differ in their specific properties. Some existences have properties that are impossible for others, like the example of lights: the light of the sun has properties (e.g., causing heat, light) that the light of a fire does not, even though both are called 'light'. Similarly, the absolute term 'existence' is predicated of all existents as a non-essential concomitant (lazim ghayr dhati), not as a genus or essence. Therefore, the properties of the Necessary Being's existence are unique to Him.

Key Terms
طبيعة نوعية
لوازم
وجودات
أنوار
لازم غير ذاتي
مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'existence' is a univocal term that applies identically to all existents.
Correction: Al-Taftazani shows that existence is an analogical term; existences are similar in reality but have different concomitants, and the absolute is predicated non-essentially.

Checkpoint

B) The essence and attributes of God
C) The human and animal species
D) The Necessary and contingent beings

Key Takeaway
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that existence is a univocal genus by showing that existences share a similar reality but differ in their specific properties, analogous to lights; the absolute 'existence' is a non-essential concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Write down one specific situation today where you will remind yourself that no creature shares Allah’s mode of existence.
?
The source denies that existence is a 'generic nature'. Where in your heart do you still treat Allah’s existence as just 'more of the same'—bigger, stronger, but not fundamentally other?  If you truly believed this, how would your fear of Allah change right now?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
10 min
Existence is Analogical, Not a Generic Nature
The author refutes the claim that existence is a univocal nature and demonstrates tashkik.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why existence, despite being a single concept, is predicated on things by way of gradation (tashkik) rather than as a univocal genus.

Core Idea
Existence is one in concept but gradational in reality—it is not a genus.

The question of whether existence is a genus or something else is central to Islamic metaphysics. If existence were a univocal genus common to God and creatures, then God and creatures would share in the same essential reality, which is impossible. This lesson examines the author's argument that existence is predicated by tashkik (gradation), not by tawatu' (univocity), and how this refutes Imam al-Razi's objection.

Guided Path
1
Understand the problem: Is existence univocal?
Read the passage to see why some philosophers mistakenly thought existence is a generic nature shared equally by all existents.
2
Learn the concept of tashkik (gradation)
The author argues that existence is predicated by tashkik: it is more prior in the cause, more deserving in substances, and more intense in stable accidents. Identify these levels.
Tashkik means that the same concept applies to things in different degrees of priority, intensity, or perfection.
3
See how the author refutes al-Razi's objection
Al-Razi's dilemma claimed that if existence implies inherence, God and creatures are equal; if not, God's necessity comes from another. The author shows this confusion arises from conflating conceptual equality with real equality.
4
Grasp the distinction between conceptual and real equality
The author emphasizes that equality in the concept of existence does not entail equality in reality or in necessary accidents. This is the key to understanding tashkik.
Conceptual univocity does not imply real univocity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كالبياض على البياضات والحرارة على الحرارات فلا يلزم من كون الوجود مفهوماً واحداً مشتركاً بين الوجودات كونه طبيعة نوعية والوجودات أفراداً متفقة الحقيقة واللوازم وإن فرضنا اشتراك الكل في مفهوم الوجود على السواء، من غير أولية ولا أولوية، إلا أنه لما كان الواقع هو التشكيك وكان من دأب الحكيم المحقق سلوك طريق التحقيق ذكر في جواب استدلالات الإمام أن الوجود مقول على الوجودات بالتشكيك، لأنه في العلة أقدم منه في المعلول وفي الجواهر أولى منه في العرض، وفي العرض القار كالسواد أشد منه في غير القار كالحركة، بل هو في الواجب أقدم وأولى وأشد منه في الممكن، والواقع على الأشياء بالتشكيك يكون عارضاً لها خارجاً عنها، لا ماهية لها أو جزء ماهية لامتناع اختلافهما على ما سيأتي، فلا يكون الوجود طبيعة نوعية للوجودات، بل لازماً خارجاً يقع على ما تحته بمعنى واحد، ولا يلزم من تساوي ملزوماته التي هي وجود الواجب ووجودات الممكنات في الحقيقة ليمتنع اختلافها في العروض واللاعروض، وفي المبدئية للممكنات وعدم المبدئية إلى غير ذلك. والعجب أن الإمام قد اطلع من كلام الفارابي وابن سينا على أن مرادهم أن حقيقة الواجب وجود مجرد، هي محض الواجبية لاشتراك فيه أصلاً، والوجود المشترك العام المعلوم لازم له غير مقوم بل صرح في بعض كتبه بأن الوجود مقول على الوجودات بالتشكيك ثم استمر على شبهته التي زعم أنها من المتانة بحيث لا يمكن توجيه شك نحيل عليها، وهي أن الوجود إن اقتضى العروض أو اللاعروض تساوى الواجب والممكن في ذلك، وإن لم يقتض شيئاً منهما كان وجوب الواجب من الغير. وجملة الأمر أنه لم يفرق بين التساوي في المفهوم والتساوي في الحقيقة، فذهب إلى أنه لا بد أحد الأمرين، إما كون اشتراك الوجود لفظياً أو كون الوجودات متساوية في اللوازم.
Like whiteness upon whitenesses and heat upon heats, it does not follow from existence being one concept shared among existences that it is a generic nature and existences are individuals agreeing in reality and necessary accidents, even if we assume the sharing of all in the concept of existence equally, without priority or superiority. However, since the reality is gradation (tashkik) and it is the practice of the veracious philosopher to follow the path of investigation, he mentioned in response to the Imam's arguments that existence is predicated upon existences by way of gradation (tashkik), because it is in the cause more prior than in the effect, in substances more deserving than in accidents, and in the stable accident like blackness more intense than in the unstable like motion; rather, it is in the Necessary (al-wajib) more prior, more deserving, and more intense than in the contingent (al-mumkin). What is predicated upon things by gradation is an accident (arid) external to them, not their essence or a part of their essence, because of the impossibility of their differing, as will come. So existence is not a generic nature for existences, but rather an external necessary accident (lazim) predicated upon what is below it with one meaning, and it does not follow from the equality of its substrata, which are the existence of the Necessary and the existences of contingents, in reality that they must be prevented from differing in inherence and non-inherence, and in being origination for contingents and not being origination, etc. It is remarkable that the Imam had seen from the words of al-Fārābī and Ibn Sīnā that their intent is that the reality of the Necessary is pure existence, which is sheer necessity (al-wājibiyya) with no sharing in it at all, and the common shared existence known is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of it, not constitutive (muqawwim); rather, he stated in some of his books that existence is predicated upon existences by gradation. Then he persisted in his doubt which he claimed was of such soundness that no subtle doubt could be directed against it, namely: if existence necessitates inherence or non-inherence, then the Necessary and the contingent are equal in that; if it necessitates neither, then the necessity of the Necessary comes from another. The gist of the matter is that he did not distinguish between equality in concept and equality in reality, so he concluded that one of two things is unavoidable: either the sharing of existence is verbal (lafẓī) or existences are equal in necessary accidents (lawāzim).
Source pages p. 320

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection by Imam al-Rāzī regarding the nature of existence. The author begins with an example: just as whiteness is predicated on many white things and heat on many hot things without making them identical in essence, so too does existence being a single concept not imply a generic nature. He then argues that in reality existence is gradational (mushakkak): it is more prior in the cause, more deserving in substances, more intense in stable accidents, and most of all in the Necessary Being. This gradational predication means existence is an accidental concomitant external to essences, not a genus. The author criticizes the Imam for failing to distinguish between conceptual equality (al-tasāwī fī al-mafhūm) and real essential equality (al-tasāwī fī al-ḥaqīqa). The Imam's doubt is presented: if existence implies inherence (al-ʿurūḍ) then God and creatures are equal; if not, then God's necessity comes from another. The author shows this dilemma is false because it conflates the conceptual with the real.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Existence being a single concept means all existences share the same reality and are equal in essence.
Correction: Existence is one in concept but gradational in reality; differences in priority, intensity, and necessity show it is a concomitant, not a genus.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the predication of existence according to gradation (tashkik)?
A) It makes existence a generic nature for all existences.
B) It makes existence an external accident that differs in priority and intensity across existences.
C) It implies all existences are equal in their necessary accidents.
D) It forces a choice between verbal equivocity and full equality.

Key Takeaway
Existence is predicated upon existences by way of gradation, not as a univocal genus; it is an external concomitant that varies in priority and intensity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the gradation of existence—from unstable accidents to the Necessary Being—where do you place your own existence? What does your heart cling to as 'necessary' for your happiness? How does the recognition that only Allah's existence is truly necessary challenge you to reorient your dependencies?
?
You treat many things as if they share existence equally with Allah—your health, wealth, relationships. Which one of these, if removed, would make you question Allah's necessity? What would it take for you to see them as the contingent, derived existences they are, and to let go of the illusion that they are independent?

Preventing Equality of Necessary and Contingent Existence in Essence
Refutation of objections to gradational existence by denying equality in essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author’s argument that the existence of God and contingent beings are not equal in complete essence, even if they share the abstract concept of existence.

Core Idea
God’s existence is unique and not merely an addition to an essence.

In this passage, the author tackles a potential objection to the gradational view of existence. If existence is gradational, does it not add to every essence uniformly? The author clarifies that while contingent existences are accidents added to essences, God's existence is self-subsistent and fundamentally different.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Pay attention to the objection and the author's refutation.
2
Identify the two objections
The author responds to two claims: one based on univocal existence and one on gradational existence. Find them.
3
Understand the core answer
The key is that God's existence is qayyūm—self-subsistent—so it is not added to an essence like contingent existences.
4
Reflect on the quote from al-Mawāqif
The quote summarizes the answer: prevent equality in complete essence, though they share an accidental concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
منع تساوي وجودي الواجب والممكن في الماهية (قال: فيزيد عليها ولا يستلزم زيادتها على ماهياتها). دفع لما سبق إلى بعض الأوهام من أن الوجود إذا كان مشككاً كان زائداً على الكل وهو المطلوب، حتى قالوا إن اختلافه في العروض واللاعروض على تقدير التواطؤ محال، وعلى تقدير التشكيك تهافت لاستلزامه العروض في الكل، فنقول: كلاهما فاسد. أما الأول: فلما سبق من أن المتواطىء قد لا يكون ذاتياً لما تحته بل عارضاً تختلف معروضاته بالحقيقة واللوازم. وأما الثاني: فلأن كون الوجود مشككاً إنما يستلزم زيادته على ما تحته من الوجودات وهو غير مطلوب، والمطلوب زيادة الوجودات الخاصة بها بأن يكون كل منها عارضاً لماهية قائماً بها في العقل، وهو غير لازم، لجواز أن يكون أحد معروضات مفهوم موجود أو المشكك وجوداً قيوماً، أي قائماً بنفسه مقيماً لغيره، لكونه حقيقة مخالفة لسائر المعروضات. وأما تعجب الإمام: بأن العرض الذي بلغ في الضعف إلى حيث لا يستقل بالمفهومية والمحكومية لكونه أمراً إضافياً وهو الكون في الأعيان، كيف صار في حق الواجب ذاتاً (قال صاحب المواقف: حقيقة الجواب هو منع تساوي وجودي الواجب والممكن في تمام الماهية وإن كانا متشاركين في عارض صادق عليهما هو مفهوم الوجود المطلق، سواء كان صدقه عليهما تواطؤاً أو تشكيكاً). (المواقف ج ١ ص ١٦١).
Preventing equality of the existence of the Necessary and the contingent in essence. (He said: Then it [existence] adds to them and does not entail its addition to their essences.) This is a repulsion of what occurred to some minds that if existence is gradational, it is additional to everything, which is what is sought, until they said that its difference in inherence and non-inherence is impossible on the assumption of univocity (tawāṭuʾ) and contradictory on the assumption of gradation because it entails inherence in everything. So we say: Both are false. As for the first: because of what preceded, that the univocal may not be essential to what is under it but rather an accident whose subjects differ in reality and necessary accidents. As for the second: because existence being gradational only entails its addition to what is under it of existences, which is not what is sought; what is sought is the addition of the particular existences to their own essences, such that each is an accident of an essence subsistent therein in the mind, and that is not necessary, because it is possible that one of the subjects of the concept of existence or the gradational be a self-subsistent existence (wujūd qayyūm), i.e., subsistent by itself and sustaining others, because it is a reality different from all other subjects. As for the Imam's wonderment: how an accident that has reached such weakness that it cannot independently perform conceptuality and predicability because it is a relational matter—namely, being-in-the-world (al-kawn fī al-aʿyān)—has become in the case of the Necessary a self-subsistent essence (dhāt). (Sāḥib al-Mawāqif said: The reality of the answer is to prevent the equality of the existence of the Necessary and the contingent in complete essence, even if they share an accident true of both, which is the concept of absolute existence, whether its truth to them is by univocity or gradation.) (al-Mawāqif, vol. 1, p. 161).
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents the author's core answer to the problem posed by Imam al-Rāzī. The answer is to deny (manʿ) that the existence of God and the existence of contingent beings are equal in their complete essence (al-māhiyya). The author first states an objection: someone might say that if existence is gradational (mushakkak), then it adds to every existence, which is exactly the required conclusion (that existence adds to essences). The author rejects this objection by distinguishing between two senses of 'addition'. The first objection (that univocal existence must be essential) is countered by noting that a univocal concept can be accidental, not essential. The second objection (that gradational existence entails inherence in all) is countered by pointing out that gradational existence only adds to the existences under it (i.e., to contingent existences), but does not require that each particular existence be an accident added to an essence. It is possible that one of the subjects of the gradational concept is a self-subsistent existence (wujūd qayyūm) like God, which is a reality entirely different from other subjects. The author ends by quoting al-Mawāqif, which affirms the same answer: the prevention of equality in complete essence, even if they share the accidental concept of absolute existence.

Misconception: Gradational existence (mushakkak) implies that existence is an extra attribute added to all essences equally, even God's.
Correction: The author explains that gradational existence only adds to the particular existences under it (contingents), but God's existence is self-subsistent and not an added accident.

C) Because equality of existence in inherence is impossible.

Key Takeaway
The existence of God and contingent beings are not equal in complete essence; God’s existence is self-subsistent and fundamentally different.

What would it take to truly let go of that comparison and worship Him as the wujūd qayyūm?
?
Since all contingent existence depends on the Self-Subsistent for every moment of its being, where in your daily life do you cling to created things as if they were self-sufficient? When fear or anxiety strikes, do you instinctively run to contingent supports instead of the one who alone subsists by Himself? Will you commit to a specific practice this week that shifts your reliance from creation to the Creator?

Refuting the Dilemma: Existence as Univocal or Equivocal
Al-Taftazani refutes the claim that existence must be either univocal or equivocal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes the claim that existence must be either univocal or equivocal, and why both positions are insufficient.

Core Idea
Existence is not forced into being univocal or equivocal; the Necessary Being is unique.

In Islamic theology, a key question is whether divine existence and contingent existence are of the same kind or radically different. Some argued that existence must be either univocal (predicated in the same sense) or equivocal (predicated in different senses). In this passage, al-Taftazani refutes both options, showing that the dilemma is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Allow the argument to unfold without jumping to conclusions.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the opponent's claim
Find where the opponent states that existence must be either univocal or equivocal. Notice the reasoning behind it.
3
Understand the two possibilities
Recognize that 'univocal' means predicated in the same sense, 'equivocal' in different senses. The opponent thinks one must be true.
4
Examine the refutation of univocity
Al-Taftazani argues that univocal terms can be accidental, so univocity does not avoid the problem.
5
Examine the refutation of equivocity
He argues that equivocity does not entail that existence is an accident for all beings; the Necessary Being is self-subsistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أن الوجود إن اقتضى العروض أو اللاعروض تساوى الواجب والممكن في ذلك، وإن لم يقتض شيئاً منهما كان وجوب الواجب من الغير. وجملة الأمر أنه لم يفرق بين التساوي في المفهوم والتساوي في الحقيقة، فذهب إلى أنه لا بد أحد الأمرين، إما كون اشتراك الوجود لفظياً أو كون الوجودات متساوية في اللوازم. منع تساوي وجودي الواجب والممكن في الماهية. قال: فيزيد عليها ولا يستلزم زيادتها على ماهياتها. دفع لما سبق إلى بعض الأوهام من أن الوجود إذا كان مشككاً كان زائداً [الكثير؟] وهو المطلوب، حتى قالوا إن اختلافه في العروض واللاعروض على تقدير التواطؤ محال، وعلى تقدير التشكيك تهافت لاستلزامه العروض في الكل، فنقول: كلاهما فاسد. أما الأول: فلما سبق من أن المتواطىء قد لا يكون ذاتياً لما تحته بل عارضاً تختلف معروضاته بالحقيقة واللوازم. وأما الثاني: فلأن كون الوجود مشككاً إنما يستلزم زيادته على ما تحته من الوجودات وهو غير مطلوب، والمطلوب زيادة الوجودات الخاصة بها بأن يكون كل منها عارضاً لماهية قائماً بها في العقل، وهو غير لازم، لجواز أن يكون أحد معروضات مفهوم موجود أو المشكك وجوداً قيوماً، أي قائماً بنفسه مقيماً لغيره، لكونه حقيقة مخالفة لسائر المعروضات.
And it is that if existence necessitates attribution or non-attribution, then the Necessary and the Possible are equal in that; and if it necessitates neither, then the necessity of the Necessary is from another. The sum of the matter is that he did not distinguish between equality in concept and equality in reality, so he concluded that one of two things must be: either the sharing of existence is verbal, or the existences are equal in concomitants. He denied the equality of the two existences of the Necessary and the Possible in quiddity. He said: it adds to them and does not entail their increase over their quiddities. This is a repulsion of what occurred to some imaginations that if existence is equivocal, it is additional to all, which is the desired thing, until they said that its difference in attribution and non-attribution on the assumption of univocity is impossible, and on the assumption of equivocity is contradictory because it necessitates attribution in all. So we say: both are false. As for the first: because of what preceded, that the univocal may not be essential for what is under it but rather accidental, whose substrates differ in reality and concomitants. As for the second: because the fact that existence is equivocal only entails its addition to what is under it of existences, which is not the desired thing; the desired thing is the addition of the particular existences themselves by each being an accident for a quiddity subsisting by it in the intellect, which is not necessary, for it is possible that one of the substrates of the concept 'existent' or 'equivocal' is a self-subsistent existence, i.e., subsisting by itself and establishing others, because it is a reality different from all other substrates.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific argument regarding the nature of existence. It begins by stating the objection: if existence implies either being an accident (العارض) or not being an accident (اللاعروض), then the Necessary Being and contingent beings would be equal in that respect, which is false. The opponent then claims that existence must be either univocal (متواطئ) meaning predicated in the same sense, or equivocal (مشكك) meaning predicated in different senses. The author refutes both possibilities. First, he argues that univocity does not prevent existence from being accidental, as univocal terms can be accidental. Second, he argues that equivocity does not entail the desired conclusion that existence is added to essences in the sense of being an accident, because one of the substrates of existence (namely, the Necessary Being) may be self-subsistent and not an accident. Thus, both horns of the dilemma are false, and the argument fails.

Objection and Answer on Distinction Between Existent Beings
Al-Taftazani responds to the objection that existent beings are distinct.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how theologians argue that conceptual sharing of existence does not entail real identity between God and creatures, by distinguishing numerical distinctness from conceptual sharing.

Core Idea
Shared concept does not mean shared reality.

This passage presents a classic objection in kalam: if 'existence' is a single concept applied to both God and creatures, then they would be identical in existence, which is false. Al-Taftazani responds by clarifying two senses of 'distinction' and then proving that existence indeed is a common meaning, without entailing composition in God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the conditional structure: 'If it is said... we say...'
2
Identify the two senses of 'distinction'
The answer splits 'distinction' into two meanings: numerical non-coincidence (factual distinction) and absence of conceptual sharing. Understand why the first is not impossible and the second is not necessary.
3
Trace the proof of the denied premise
The passage includes a proof that existence must be a shared nature. Follow the conditional: 'if existence were not a specific nature... then total distinction would be necessary... but the consequent is false'. This shows why the premise is denied.
The proof relies on the impossibility of composition in God's existence.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal import
Ask yourself: Why does this argument matter for tawhid? How does it protect against anthropomorphism or pantheism?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: فإن قيل فتتباين الوجودات به. قلنا: بمعنى عدم التصاديق [؟] الغير محال، وبمعنى عدم المشارك في مفهوم الكون غير لازم، كأفراد الماشي. إثبات المقدمة الممنوعة: لو لم يكن الوجود طبيعة نوعية هي تمام حقيقة الموجودات، لزم [التباين؟] الكلي بين الوجودات ضرورة أنها لا تشترك في [اللازم؟] أصلاً، لامتناع تركيب وجود الواجب، واللازم باطل، لما ثبت من اشتراك [ذاتي؟] الوجود معنى.
He said: If it is said that existences are distinct by it, we say: if by that is meant non-coincidence [in reference?], it is not impossible; and if by that is meant no sharing in the concept of existence, it is not necessary, like individuals of 'walking'. Proof of the denied premise: if existence were not a specific nature that is the entire reality of existents, then total [distinction?] between existences would be necessary because they share nothing [essential?] at all, due to the impossibility of composition of the existence of the Necessary; but the consequent is false, because it is established that [essential?] existence shares a meaning.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage contains an objection and its answer regarding the distinction between existent beings. The objection likely states that if existence is a single concept shared by God and creatures, then they would be identical in existence, which is false. The answer distinguishes different senses of 'distinction': if 'distinction' means numerical difference (non-coincidence), it is not impossible; if it means no sharing in the concept of existence, it is not necessary. The author then presents a proof relying on a denied premise: if existence were not a shared nature, then all existents would be totally distinct with no shared meaning, which is false because they share the concept of existence. The argument supports the view that existence is a common, though analogically applied, concept.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God and creatures share the concept of 'existence', then they must be identical in reality.
Correction: Sharing a concept does not imply identity of essence or reality; individuals within a category share a concept but are numerically distinct.

Key Takeaway

For example, do you imagine His mercy as similar to human mercy, only more? This lesson forces you to hold two truths simultaneously: the concept is shared, but the reality is incomparably different. Examine your worship—is there any area where you have unconsciously lowered Allah to a human level because of shared concepts? What step will you take tonight to rectify this?
?
If existence is a specific nature shared by all existents, then every creature's existence points back to the Creator who gave it. Yet we often live as if our existence is self-sufficient. Take a moment: when you wake up tomorrow, how will you remind yourself that your very existence is a borrowed concept from the One whose existence is necessary? Ibn Sina's View on Existence as Additional to Essence
Ibn Sina's position and responses to two objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's position that existence is additional to essence and how he defends it against two objections.

Core Idea
Existence is not the same as essence; it is a distinct addition.

One of the key debates in Islamic theology is whether existence is identical to essence or an added attribute. Ibn Sina argued for the latter, but this view faces objections about infinite regress and the nature of subsistence. In this lesson, we examine his argument and his responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: Ibn Sina's view, then two objections and their answers.
Pay attention to the analogies used.
2
Identify the two objections
The first objection concerns subsistence in mahiya; the second about the existence of existence.
Write them down in your own words.
3
Understand the responses
The first response says subsistence in mahiya as such is not impossible. The second says existence of existence is self-identical. The final part says subsistence is intellectual, so no regress.
Reflect on how these respond to the objections.
4
Reflect on the analogy
The passage compares existence to time, which is self-actualizing. Think about how this analogy supports the view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: وذهب الشيخ إلى أنه زائد، وأجيب عن الأول بأن قيامه بالماهية من حيث هي لا يقتضي المحال، وعن الثاني بأن وجود الوجود عينه، وإنما النزاع في غيره، وتحقيقه أن بالوجود تتحقق الأشياء، فيكون تحققه بنفسه كحال الزمان، مع التقدم والتأخر، على أنه لا استحالة في كونه معدوماً. ثم قال: قلنا: القيام بها عقلي فيكفي لحصولها في العقل من الغير اعتباره، وإن اعتبر فلا تسلسل في الاعتبارات.
He said: And al-Shaykh (Ibn Sina) held that it (wujud) is additional. And it was answered regarding the first (objection) that its subsistence (qiyām) in the essence (māhiya) as such does not entail impossibility; and regarding the second that the existence of existence is identical to itself, and the dispute is only concerning other things. Its verification is that by existence things become actual, so its actualization is by itself like the case of time, with priority and posteriority, and there is no impossibility in it being non-existent. Then he said: We say: Subsistence in it is intellectual (ʿaqlī), so it suffices for its obtaining in the intellect from another that it be considered, and if considered, there is no regress in considerations.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's position that existence (wujud) is additional to essence (mahiya) and then addresses two objections. The first objection claimed that if wujud subsists in mahiya, it leads to absurdity (regress or contradiction). The response: subsistence in mahiya as such is not impossible because mahiya can be considered as a subject without requiring prior existence. The second objection argued that if wujud is additional, then the existence of wujud itself would require another existence ad infinitum. The response: the existence of existence is self-identical; the dispute concerns other things. The analogy of time illustrates self-actualization. Then the author adds that this subsistence is intellectual, not external, so no infinite regress occurs in mental considerations.

Key Terms
الوجود (wujūd)
الماهية (māhiya)
القيام (qiyām)
العقلي (ʿaqlī)
التسلسل (tasalsul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is additional to essence, it leads to an infinite regress because each existence would need another existence.
Correction: The author argues that the existence of existence is self-identical, and the subsistence is intellectual, so no actual regress occurs.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection that if wujud is additional to mahiya, it leads to regress?
A) By denying that wujud is additional.
B) By saying the subsistence is intellectual, not external.
C) By asserting that wujud is identical to mahiya.
D) By claiming that regress is acceptable.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina defended the addition of existence to essence by showing that the supposed regress is avoided because existence is self-actualizing and subsistence is intellectual.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your existence is not the same as your essence, then you are not necessary—you could have not existed. Do you live as if your existence is a gift from another, or do you take it for granted? How might you change your daily actions to reflect this gift?
?
Ibn Sina says existence is self-actualizing for itself, but for you it is added. This means your essence does not guarantee your existence. Are there areas in your life where you act as if your existence is self-generated, ignoring your dependence on the Necessary Being? What would it look like to consciously rely on Him for your next breath?
?
The subsistence of existence in essence is intellectual—it's a mental consideration. Does this distinction affect how you view your own identity? Are you more than your thoughts about yourself? How can you move from just 'considering' your existence to actually living in awareness of the One who grants it?

Two Proofs that Existence is Identical to Essence
Sanusi presents the first two of four proofs against the additionality of existence to essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key arguments that existence (wujud) cannot be additional to essence (mahiya), and why subsistence (qiyam) requires the subject to exist.

Core Idea
Existence cannot subsist in a nonexistent — that would combine existence and nonexistence.

This passage presents the first two of four proofs that existence is identical to essence in both the Necessary and the contingent. The arguments focus on the logical impossibility of an attribute subsisting in a subject that does not itself exist. We will examine the first two proofs and the underlying reasoning about subsistence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once, noting the technical terms wujud, mahiya, qiyam, dawr, tasalsul.
2
Understand the premise
The proponents assume subsistence requires the subject to be established in itself (thabita fi nafsiha). If wujud is additional to mahiya, it must subsist in it.
3
Analyze the first proof
The first proof: if wujud subsists in mahiya while mahiya is nonexistent without wujud, then wujud subsists in a nonexistent, which is a contradiction.
Combining existence and nonexistence in the same subject is impossible.
4
Analyze the second proof
The second proof: subsistence requires priority of existence of the subject. If the essence has existence prior to wujud, either that prior existence is wujud itself (circular) or another (regress).
Both circularity and regress are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج القائلون بكون الوجود نفس الماهية في الواجب والممكنات جميعاً بأنه لو لم يكن الوجود نفس الماهية وليس جزءاً منها بالاتفاق لكان زائداً عليها قائماً بها قيام الصفة بالموصوف، وقيام الشيء بالشيء فرع ثبوتهما في نفسيهما، لأن ما لا كون له في نفسه لا يكون قائماً ولا في محل. وهذا بالنظر إلى الوجود والماهية ممنوع؛ أما في جانب الماهية فلأنها بالوجود تتحقق محلاً للوجود، ولكن ذلك التحقق إما بذلك الوجود فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وإما بوجود آخر فيلزم التسلسل. وأما في جانب الوجود فلأنه لو تحقق زائداً عليها لتسلسلت الوجودات. فتمكن الاحتجاج على امتناع زيادة الوجود على الماهية بأربعة أوجه: الأول: أنه لو قام بها وهي بدون الوجود معدومة لزم قيام الوجود بالمعدوم، وفيه جمع بين صفتي الوجود والعدم، وهو تناقض. الثاني: أنه لو قام بها لزم سبقها بالوجود كما في سائر المعروضات، فإن كان لذلك الموجودية هو الوجود الأول لزم الدور، وإن كان غيره لزم التسلسل.
Those who hold that existence is identical to essence in both the Necessary and the contingent argue: if existence were not identical to essence and is not a part of it by agreement, then it would be additional to it, subsisting in it as an attribute subsists in its subject. And the subsistence of a thing in a thing is dependent on their being established in themselves, because that which has no existence in itself cannot subsist nor be in a subject. But this is denied with respect to existence and essence: as for the essence, it becomes actualized by existence as a locus for existence, yet that actualization is either by that existence, entailing the priority of a thing over itself, or by another existence, entailing regress. As for existence, if it were actualized as additional to essence, existences would regress. Thus one can argue for the impossibility of existence being additional to essence by four aspects: First: if it subsists in essence while it (essence) is nonexistent without existence, then existence would subsist in a nonexistent, combining the attributes of existence and nonexistence, which is a contradiction. Second: if it subsists in it, it would require the priority of existence over it as in other subjects; if that existence is the first existence, circularity follows, and if it is another, regress follows.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is a classification: four proofs that existence (wujud) cannot be additional to essence (mahiya). The proponents of identity argue from the impossibility of subsistence (qiyam) of an accident in a subject that does not itself exist. They examine two sides: the essence side and the existence side. If wujud is additional, the essence must exist to receive it, leading to either self-priority or regress. Similarly, wujud itself would need a prior existence. The first proof: wujud subsisting in a nonexistent essence is contradictory. The second proof: if it subsists, the essence must be prior in existence, causing either circularity or regress. The third and fourth proofs are not fully preserved in the text but are implied to follow similar logic.

Key Terms
الوجود (wujūd)
الماهية (māhiya)
القيام (qiyām)
الدور (dawr)
التسلسل (tasalsul)
الصفة (ṣifa)
الموصوف (mawṣūf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that subsistence (qiyam) can occur even if the subject is nonexistent in external reality, as long as it exists mentally.
Correction: The proofs reject this by requiring the subject to be 'established in itself' (thabita fi nafsiha), which means real, not merely mental, existence. A mental nonexistent cannot be a locus for a real attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the logical problem if existence (wujud) is additional to a nonexistent essence?
A) It leads to an infinite number of existences.
B) Existence would subsist in a nonexistent, which is contradictory.
C) The essence would be both existent and nonexistent.
D) It violates the law of non-contradiction in a different way.

Key Takeaway
Existence cannot be an additional attribute of essence because it would require subsistence in a nonexistent or lead to circularity/regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah's essence is identical to His existence, then He is utterly simple and dependent on nothing. In what areas of your life do you act as if something else is needed to complete His reality—like trusting wealth, status, or people as if they add to His provision? Commit today to one concrete situation where you will rely solely on Allah's sufficiency.
?
Sanusi shows that adding existence to essence leads to absurdity. Your own existence is a gift from Allah, not an added layer to your identity. Are you living as if your worth is separate from your purpose to worship Him? What 'addition' in your life—a particular attachment, habit, or ambition—needs to be stripped away so that your existence reflects pure servitude to Allah? Make a plan to remove it today.
?
The proofs rely on the impossibility of a nonexistent being a subject for existence. Think about your moments of heedlessness: when you forget Allah, it is as if you are trying to give reality to something that has no true existence. How can you use the truth of this lesson to remind yourself that only Allah truly Is, and that everything else derives its existence from Him? Write one phrase from this lesson you will repeat when you feel distracted.

Existence is Existent and Self-Actualizing
Al-Taftazani argues that existence is existent and self-actualizing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Taftazani's argument that existence is existent and self-actualizing, and how it avoids the need for an infinite regress.

Core Idea
Existence actualizes itself; it does not need another existence to subsist.

Al-Taftazani responds to the objection that existence must be either existent or non-existent. He first defends the position that existence is existent, arguing that since everything is actualized by existence, existence itself must be self-actualizing. This lesson examines his reasoning and the distinction between self-necessary existence and self-actualization.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on existence being existent
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Focus on the claim that existence is existent and the initial justification.
The text contains some unclear phrases; rely on the translation and commentary for clarity.
2
Understand the analogy with time
Identify the analogy between existence and time: just as priority and posteriority in time are inherent in its parts without needing another time, existence actualizes itself without needing another existence.
3
Examine the objection and response
Notice the objection that if existence is existent, then every existence would be necessary. Al-Taftazani responds by distinguishing between 'self-necessary' (wajib bi al-dhat) and 'self-actualization', showing that the latter applies to both necessary and possible beings.
The response also notes a looseness in the phrase 'actualization of things by existence'—existence is the actualization itself, not an independent cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعن الثاني : أنا نختار أن الوجود موجود، ولا يلزم التخلف والمآل [؟] ويلزم لو كان الوجود أيضاً عارضاً عليه، وليس كذلك بل وجوده عينه أو إثمار [؟] النواع في غيره، والأدلة إنما قامت عليه وتحقيق ذلك أنه لما كان تحقق كل شيء بالوجود، فبالضرورة يكون تحققه بنفسه، من غير احتياج إلى وجود آخر يقوم به. كما أنه لما كان التقدم والتأخر فيما بين الأشياء بالزمان كائناً فيما بين أجزائه بالذات من غير افتقار إلى زمان آخر [؟] ساعة [؟] بين [؟] له [؟] لي [؟] سالم [؟] ان [؟] كالحمار [؟] فإن قيل ليكون كل وجود واجباً إذ لا معنى لها سوى التي يكون [؟] والحق [؟] بنفسه [؟] المسان [؟] أريد [؟] سيا [؟] ه [؟] ان [؟] با [؟] دقلنا ممنوع فإن معنى وجود الواجب النفسي أنه لمقتضى ذاته من غير احتياج إلى فاعل، ومعنى تحقق الوجود بنفسه أنه إذا حصل للشيء إما من ذاته كما في الواجب، أو من غيره كما في الممكن لم يفتقر تحققه إلى وجود آخر يقوم به بخلاف الإنسان فإنه إنما يتحقق بعد تأثير الفاعل بوجود يقوم به [؟] العقلان على أن في قولنا تحقق الأشياء بالوجود تسامحاً في العبارة، إذ الوجود نفس التحقق لا أمرية تحققها، والمعنى أن تحقق الأشياء يكون عند قيام الوجود بها [؟] العقل [؟] واتحادها به هوية [؟] أو نختار أن الوجود معدوم ولا يلزم [؟] اتصاف بنقيضه، بمعنى صدقه عليه، لأن نقيض الوجود هو العدم واللا وجود لا المعدوم، ولا اللاموجود، فغاية الأمر أنه يلزم أن الوجود ليس بذي وجود، كما أن السواد ليس بذي سواد، والأمر كذلك، ولا يلزم أيضاً أن يتحقق في المحل ما لا تحقق له في نفسه لما عرفت من أن قيام الموجود بالماهية ليس بحسب الخارج، كقيام البياض بالجسم، بل بحسب العقل فلا يلزم إلا تحققه في [؟] سئلة [؟] سنه [؟] (1) يجاب عن الأول بأنه منقوض بالأعراض القائمة بالمحال كسواد [؟] العمل [؟] وقد.
As for the second: we choose that existence is existent, and no delay or outcome [?] follows, and it would follow if existence were also an accident upon it, but it is not so rather its existence is the same as it or fruiting [?] of kinds in others, and the proofs are established upon it. The verification of that is that since everything is actualized by existence, then necessarily its actualization is by itself, without need for another existence to subsist in it. Just as when priority and posteriority among things by time is inherent among its parts by essence without need for another time [?] hour [?] between [?] for [?] to [?] sound [?] that [?] like a donkey [?] For if it is said: let every existence be necessary, for there is no meaning except that which is [??] and truth [??] by itself [??] the question [??] I mean [??] [??] [??] [??] [??] We say: it is prevented. For the meaning of the existence of the self-necessary is that it is by the requirement of its essence without need for an agent, and the meaning of the actualization of existence by itself is that when it occurs to a thing either from itself as in the necessary, or from another as in the possible, its actualization does not need another existence to subsist in it, unlike the human, for he is only actualized after the agent's effect by an existence subsisting in him [??] the two intellects [??] on that in our statement 'actualization of things by existence' there is a looseness of expression, for existence is the actualization itself, not a thing that actualizes, and the meaning is that the actualization of things is when existence subsists in them [??] the intellect [??] and their unity with it as identity [??] Or we choose that existence is non-existent and no [??] follows, description by its opposite, meaning its truth upon it, because the opposite of existence is non-existence and not-existence, not the non-existent, nor the not-existent, so the utmost is that it follows that existence is not possessor of existence, just as blackness is not possessor of blackness, and the matter is so. And it does not also follow that something which has no actualization in itself is actualized in a locus, because you have known that the subsistence of the existent in essence is not according to external, like the subsistence of whiteness in body, but according to intellect, so only its actualization follows in [??] question [??] year [??] (1) It is answered about the first that it is refuted by the accidents subsisting in impossible [loci] like blackness [??] the deed [??] and it has.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage addresses the second objection concerning whether existence itself exists or not. The author chooses that existence is existent, arguing that since everything is actualized by existence, existence itself must be self-actualizing without need for another existence. This is defended against the objection that would make every existence necessary. The response distinguishes between the meaning of the self-necessary (wajib bi al-dhat) and self-actualization: the latter can apply to both necessary and possible beings without requiring another substrate. The author notes a looseness in the phrase 'actualization of things by existence'—existence is actualization, not an independent entity. Alternatively, one may choose that existence is non-existent, in which case it would not be predicated with existence, just as blackness is not black. This does not lead to absurdity because the subsistence in question is intellectual, not external. The passage ends with a note about an objection being refuted by accidents in impossible subjects.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If existence is existent, it would need another existence to exist, leading to an infinite regress.
Correction: Existence is self-actualizing; it does not require another existence to subsist, just as time's parts do not need another time.

Checkpoint
